---
title: "HOWTO: Purge and Reinstall Grub 2 from the Live CD"
date: 2010-09-24
forum: Tutorials
---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-09-24
[SIZE=4][COLOR="DarkRed"][CENTER]**HOWTO: Purge and Reinstall Grub 2 from the Live CD**[/CENTER][/COLOR][/SIZE]

There are several ways to repair Grub 2. In this post, we will completely remove and then reinstall Grub 2 using an [_Ubuntu Desktop Installation CD_]("http://www.ubuntu.com/desktop/get-ubuntu/download"), which I will refer to as the Live CD.

***Note: If you can get to your normal installation without using a Live CD the chroot procedure is not necessary and you need only Steps 2-5.***

I would recommend this procedure only after running *meierfra's* [_boot info script_]("http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net/") and finding no obvious discrepancies. The reasons for this include:
[LIST]
[*]During this procedure, you will temporarily have no bootloader on your system.
[*]If you designated the wrong drive/partition during the initial install, you may make the same mistake again.
[*]You will lose any customizations you have made to your Grub 2 configuration files.
[*]Other methods, such as "grub-install" or "grub-install --reinstall" are less intrusive on your system. Doing the least necessary to fix a system is generally a good idea.
[/LIST]
[COLOR="DarkRed"]This thread is primarily for reference.[/COLOR] If you need help interpreting the output of the boot info script, please start your own thread in one of the other forums. Depending on your experience level, [_Absolute Beginners_]("http://ubuntuforums.org/forumdisplay.php?f=326") or [_Installation & Upgrades_]("http://ubuntuforums.org/forumdisplay.php?f=333") might be appropriate forums.

**[COLOR="DarkRed"][SIZE=4]What can I try first?[/SIZE][/COLOR]**

**Boot Repair (GUI)**
There is a GUI application created by forum member *YannBuntu*that can, among other things, reinstall Grub2 (rewrite to the MBR and generate a new core.img file), or to completely purge and reinstall Grub 2 in the Advanced Options (you will still have to enter one command in the terminal). The "Boot Repair" information for now can be found in Post #171 of this thread:
[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=10871917#post10871917]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=10871917#post10871917")

**From the Normal OS (not Live CD).**
[LIST]
[*]Change the BIOS boot order. If you installed Grub 2 from another device, changing the boot order to the original installing device may find a bootable Grub installation. Once booted and in your normal Ubuntu system, run "sudo dpkg-reconfigure grub-pc" and select additional devices on which to write Grub 2. Example: If you installed to your interneal hard drive from a USB drive, change the BIOS to boot from the USB drive first. If Ubuntu now boots, run the dpkg-reconfigure command and be sure to include the internal drive in the "Grub install devices" options.

[*]Reinstalling Grub 2 may be another option. A reinstall will rewrite the MBR and refresh the modules in the *boot/grub* folder. The "update-grub" command should be run as root after installing Grub 2, as the grub.cfg file is not generated by the installation.

[*]Replace the **X** value (sd**a**, sd**b**, etc).  This is normally the *drive* with the Ubuntu OS. This should be the drive the computer's BIOS boots. The command assumes the */boot* folder is not on a separate partition. These commands should not be run from a Live CD.
```

sudo grub-install --recheck /dev/sd**X**
sudo update-grub

```
[/LIST]
**From the Live CD:**

**[COLOR="DarkRed"]Important Note on Using a LiveCD:[/COLOR]**
Please use a LiveCD with the same version of Grub 2 as the OS if you are going to run "grub-install" without 'chrooting'. Not doing so may break or fail to fix the problem.  Natty uses Grub 1.99RC. Lucid/Maverick use Grub 1.98. If Grub files are to be copied or installed to the actual OS, users of Lucid/Maverick should use a Lucid or Maverick LiveCD to repair Grub. Natty users should use a Natty LiveCD. *Once 'chrooted' into the real installation, the installation's repositories will be used if packages are downloaded and installed regardless of the CD.*

[LIST]
[*]**If the Ubuntu OS cannot be accessed through the normal boot process** (see above), boot a compatible installation CD/Live CD. The Grub 2 files are by default installed in the partition's /boot/grub folder and will be placed in this folder if the commands are run as instructed. 

Note 1: Running "update-grub" from the Live CD will *not* update the grub.cfg file of the normal installation. If the grub.cfg file is damaged or missing, the user should use the *chroot* procedure below to update the grub.cfg file.

Note 2: In the following commands, the Ubuntu *partition* (sd**a1**, sd**a5**, etc) is mounted in the first command, but *only the drive* (sd**a**, sd**b**, etc) is designated in the 'grub-install' command.

Note 3: The middle commands are to be run *only if* the operating system has a separate partition specifically for /boot. This would normally not be the case.

If you have a separate /boot partition run these two commands first. Most users do not have a separate /boot partition. *sd**XZ*** is the boot partition.
```

[COLOR="DarkRed"]### Run these two commands only if you have a separate /boot partition
sudo mkdir /mnt/boot
sudo mount /dev/sd**XZ** /mnt/boot  # Example: sudo mount /dev/sd*a6* /mnt/boot
[/COLOR]

```

```

sudo mount /dev/sd**XY** /mnt  # Example: sudo mount /dev/sd*a5* /mnt
sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sd**X** # Example: sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sd*a*

```
[/LIST]


**[COLOR="DarkRed"][SIZE=4]Purge & Reinstall without Chroot[/SIZE][/COLOR]**
If you wish to purge and reinstall Grub 2 from a normally running installation it is not necessary to use the *chroot* instructions. Following a normal boot (without the Live CD):
[LIST]
[*]Complete only steps 2 through 5.
[*]Include "sudo" at the start of each command.  Example: *sudo apt-get purge grub grub-pc grub-common *
[/LIST]

**[COLOR="DarkRed"][SIZE=4]Chroot[/SIZE][/COLOR]**

The following is the textual explanation of how to 'chroot' and install Grub 2 from the LiveCD. [COLOR="DarkRed"]**For an easy-to-understand summary including graphics, the following site does a very good job - complete with illustrations:**[/COLOR]
[http://opensource-sidh.blogspot.com/2011/06/recover-grub-live-ubuntu-cd.html]("http://opensource-sidh.blogspot.com/2011/06/recover-grub-live-ubuntu-cd.html")

Since you will boot from the Live CD, commands executed in the normal manner would act on the Live CD's temporary system files and not the files on your real installation's partition. By 'chroot-ing' into your Ubuntu partition, the commands will act on your installation and remain after you remove the CD and reboot. Use a LiveCD with the same version of Grub 2 (see note near the top of this post).

**Procedure: ***
Boot to the Live CD Desktop and open a terminal (Applications, Accessories, Terminal).

* If you can boot to your normal Ubuntu installation and wish to purge and reinstall Grub 2, see the section "**Purge & Reinstall without Chroot**".

A note about copying: Copying these commands is easier and more accurate than typing. To paste into a linux terminal, highlight the text with your mouse, then click in the terminal with your middle mouse button to paste the contents. It's that simple!. If you prefer to use  the keyboard, paste *into* the terminal with CTRL-*SHIFT*-V. 


[COLOR="Red"]**Important:**[/COLOR] All instances of **[COLOR="DarkRed"]sdX[/COLOR]** and **[COLOR="DarkRed"]sdXY[/COLOR]** must be replaced with the correct drive/partition for your system. (sda, sdb, sda1, sda5, sdb5, etc). **[COLOR="DarkRed"]X[/COLOR]** is the drive letter. **[COLOR="DarkRed"]Y[/COLOR]** is the partition number. The first drive is "a" and the first partition is "1". Example: sd*a1*


**[COLOR="Navy"]1. Chroot into your real system.[/COLOR]** The following set of commands will mount the necessary system files to allow the chroot and place you in a terminal where the commands will work on your real installation. Note the middle two commands are to be run *ONLY IF* you have a separate partition specifically for /boot. Copying the *resolv.conf* file may help establish an Internet connection.

Note: The "*for i in ...* line is a command that needs to be run. It is an efficient way to mount multiple items using a single line of code. I have added a section at the end of this post showing what the command does. [size=3]**[COLOR="DarkRed"]*[/COLOR]**[/size]
 
Change [COLOR="DarkRed"]sd**XY**[/COLOR] to correct value (for example, sda5, sdb1, etc.).

**[COLOR="DarkRed"]Mounting Instructions:[/COLOR]**
When mounting the partitions, use A (separate /boot partition), B (Wubi only), *or* C (normal installtion).
Once you have completed the mounting instructions in the section that applies to your installation, go to *Continue*.

[LIST=A]
[*]**[COLOR="DarkRed"]Only If You Have a Separate /boot partition.[/COLOR]**
If you have a separate /boot partition run these commands. Most users do not have a separate /boot partition and should go to the normal partition section. *sd**XY*** is the main partition. *sd**XZ*** is the boot partition.
```

sudo mkdir /mnt/temp /mnt/temp/boot  
sudo mount /dev/sd**XY** /mnt/temp  # Mount the main Ubuntu partition. Example: sudo mount /dev/sda5 /mnt/temp
sudo mount /dev/sd**XZ** /mnt/temp/boot  # Mount the /boot partition. Example: sudo mount /dev/sda6 /mnt/temp/boot

```

[*]**[COLOR="DarkRed"]Only If You Are Running WUBI (Ubuntu inside Windows)[/COLOR]**
It is possible to chroot into a Wubi install by first mounting your Windows partition, and then mounting the Windows Wubi file, *root.disk*. The user must determine the value of the Window partition (sda1, sda2, etc). This section only applies to users booting an Ubuntu OS installed within Windows.
```

sudo mkdir /mnt/windows /mnt/temp  # Make the mount points
sudo mount /dev/sd**XY** /mnt/windows  # Mount the Windows partition. Example: sudo mount /dev/sda1 /mnt/windows
sudo mount -o loop /mnt/windows/ubuntu/disks/root.disk /mnt/temp  # Mount the root.disk file.

```

[*]**[COLOR="DarkRed"]If You Have a Normal Installation.[/COLOR]** No separate /boot partition, not a Windows/Wubi installation.
```

sudo mkdir /mnt/temp 
sudo mount /dev/sd**XY** /mnt/temp  # Example: sudo mount /dev/sd*a5* /mnt/temp

```
[/LIST]

**Continue from A, B or C.**
At this point the Ubuntu installation should be mounted on /mnt/temp. The next step is to mount certain items in preparation of the chroot. The "for i in ..." is a command that will mount these items and must be run.
```

for i in /dev /dev/pts /proc /sys; do sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i;  done
sudo cp /etc/resolv.conf /mnt/temp/etc/resolv.conf  # May be required to connect to the Internet.
sudo chroot /mnt/temp

``` 
If the commands ran successfully, the terminal prompt should include "root" ( root@ubuntu:/# ), which indicates you are in the *chroot* environment. 


**[COLOR="Navy"]2. Confirm an Internet connection.[/COLOR]**[COLOR="Red"]*******[/COLOR] This is necessary to retrieve the necessary Grub packages. 
```
[COLOR="DarkRed"]
apt-get update[/COLOR]   # **[COLOR="Red"]***[/COLOR]**

``` 
[LIST]
[*][COLOR="Red"]*******[/COLOR] Unless an Internet connection can be established, do not run the Section 3 commands - the packages will not download and you will be left without a bootloader! For an alternative procedure, see "Reconfiguring Grub 2 without an Internet Connection" near the end of this post.
[*]Internet failure means that your computer cannot connect to the Internet. If *any* repositories update you may safely continue to Section 3. 
[/LIST]


**[COLOR="Navy"]3. Purge Grub 2 packages.[/COLOR]** The next command will remove *grub*, *grub-pc* (Grub 2) and *grub-common*.***** Here is what you will do:
[LIST=a]
[*]Press ENTER to continue.
[*]Read the warning during the install about removing the bootloader. TAB to highlight "<Yes>" and press ENTER.  
[/LIST]
***** If you are sure you have never had Grub legacy on the current installation you may omit "grub" from the next command (*apt-get purge grub-pc grub-common*).

```
 
[COLOR="DarkRed"]apt-get purge grub grub-pc grub-common[/COLOR]

``` 


**[COLOR="Navy"]4. Reinstall the grub packages.[/COLOR]** Here is what will happen:
[LIST=a]
[*]You will be given the opportunity to add extra kernel options to the kernel line. If you don't know, you probably don't need them ;  TAB to highlight "<OK>" and press ENTER. 
[*]Read the installation notes. TAB to "<OK>" to continue.
[*]When presented with the device option, use the UP/DN keys to select the correct *drive* (**[COLOR="DarkRed"]sdX[/COLOR]**). 
[LIST]
[*]Make sure the installation *drive* **[COLOR="DarkRed"][noparse]  [*]     /dev/sdX[/noparse][/COLOR]** has an asterisk next to it ( example: [noparse]  [*]  /dev/sda  [/noparse]). If it doesn't, highlight it and press the SPACE bar to select it. 
[*]Do not select a partition ( example: [noparse]  [ ]   /dev/sda5  [/noparse] , etc).
[/LIST]
[*]TAB to "<OK>" and press ENTER. When it has finishing the installation, you should have Grub 2 installed.
[/LIST]

```
[COLOR="DarkRed"]apt-get install grub-common grub-pc[/COLOR]
``` 


**[COLOR="Navy"]5. Update the Grub 2 files .[/COLOR]** This command shouldn't be necessary, but it won't hurt to update Grub once more before exiting.  
```
 
[COLOR="DarkRed"]update-grub [/COLOR]

``` 


**[COLOR="Navy"]6. Exit the chroot environment.[/COLOR]** 
```
[COLOR="DarkRed"]exit[/COLOR]
``` 

If you have successfully exited chroot, the terminal prompt should return to the Ubuntu Live CD prompt ( ubuntu@ubuntu:~$ )


**[COLOR="Navy"]7. Unmount what you previously mounted.*[/COLOR]**
```

for i in /dev/pts /dev /proc /sys; do sudo umount /mnt/temp$i ; done

``` 

* If you mounted a separate /boot partition, run this instead:
```

for i in /dev/pts /dev /proc /sys /boot; do sudo umount /mnt/temp$i ; done

``` 


**[COLOR="Navy"]8. Reboot your system.[/COLOR]**


**[COLOR="DarkRed"][SIZE=4]Reconfiguring Grub 2 without an Internet Connection.[/SIZE][/COLOR]**

[LIST=1]
[*]Complete Sections 1 and 2 above. If the *update-grub* command fails to connect to the Internet after *chrooting*, use the procedures below while still in the *chroot* environment.

[COLOR="Red"]***** Caution:**[/COLOR] Running the Step 3 commands above without an Internet connection will leave you without a bootloader!


[*]If you entered the *chroot* without copying the */etc/resolv.conf* file, you can try to correct the Internet problem by doing so now. The following commands will exit *chroot*, copy the additional file, and then return to the *chroot* environment.
```
[COLOR="DarkRed"]exit [/COLOR] 
sudo cp /etc/resolv.conf /mnt/temp/etc/resolv.conf
sudo chroot /mnt/temp 
[COLOR="DarkRed"]update-grub[/COLOR]
```
If you can now connect to the Internet, return to Step 3. With no connection, run only the remaining commands below.


[*]Review the notes in Step 4 above, as you will be presented with several screens awaiting user input.
```
[COLOR="DarkRed"]dpkg-reconfigure grub-pc[/COLOR]
```

[*]Update the Grub 2 files . This command shouldn't be necessary, but it won't hurt to update Grub once more before exiting.  
```
 
[COLOR="DarkRed"]update-grub [/COLOR]

``` 

[*]Exit the chroot environment: 
```
[COLOR="DarkRed"]exit[/COLOR]
``` 
If you have successfully exited chroot, the terminal prompt should return to the Ubuntu Live CD prompt ( ubuntu@ubuntu:~$ )


[*]Unmount what you previously mounted. 
```

for i in /dev/pts /dev /proc /sys ; do sudo umount /mnt/temp$i ; done

``` 

[*]Reboot your system.
[/LIST]

[size=3]**[COLOR="DarkRed"]*[/COLOR]**[/SIZE] Explanation of the "*for i in /dev /dev/pts /proc /sys; do sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i;  done*"
This command is the equivalent of:
```
sudo mount --bind /dev /mnt/temp/dev
sudo mount --bind /dev/pts /mnt/temp/dev/pts
sudo mount --bind /proc /mnt/temp/proc
sudo mount --bind /sys /mnt/temp/sys
```

### Post by holgerhom on 2010-10-14
For me, I have the boot partition on sda1 and the root partition on sda2.

In this case, I first had to mount the _root_ fs of sda2 and also /dev, /dev/pts, /proc and /sys of the root fs as described here, and then, before continuing, I _additionally_ had to mount the boot fs:

mount sda1 /mnt/temp/boot

Otherwise I got a grub prompt with no kernes found.

---

mount sda1 /mnt/temp/boot

Otherwise I got a grub prompt with no kernes found.

apt-get install grub-common grub-pc
``` the output says ```
E: Unable to locate package grub-common
E: Package 'grub-pc' has no installation candidate
```So, I'm kind of stuck at this point. I tried an ifconfig while still in chroot, and it shows connectivity, but I don't know if that means that the mounted drive has connectivity or just the live CD (or in my case Live USB drive). At any rate, I need help. Thanks.

### Post by drs305 on 2010-10-14
> **smfoote said:**
> So, I'm kind of stuck at this point. I tried an ifconfig while still in chroot, and it shows connectivity, but I don't know if that means that the mounted drive has connectivity or just the live CD (or in my case Live USB drive). At any rate, I need help. Thanks.

Did you run the apt-get command from within the chroot and did the normal repository lists sroll down the screen? If you aren't sure if your chroot has Internet, "ping google.com", CTRL-c to stop the returns.

If you don't have a connection, take a look at Section 8 about copying resolv.conf and try it again. You can also check your repository list in the *chroot* environment with:
```
cat /etc/apt/sources.list | grep -v "#"
```
Make sure you have *main* enabled.

It's ok to exit and enter the chroot environment, but don't reboot the system until we can get grub-pc reinstalled.

Added: Your profile shows Maverick, so my assumption is that you are using Grub2. You can always try running "apt-get install grub" if Grub legacy is what you are really running.

### Post by drs305 on 2010-10-14
The repositories look correct and grub-common and grub-pc are contained therein - I checked. 

Try downloading the files from this location. I don't know if your install has the dependencies already but we can see:

Maverick 32-bit:
```

wget http://mirrors.kernel.org/ubuntu/pool/main/g/grub2/grub-common_1.98+20100804-5ubuntu3_i386.deb  # grub-common
wget http://mirrors.kernel.org/ubuntu/pool/main/g/grub2/grub-pc_1.98+20100804-5ubuntu3_i386.deb  # grub-pc 32-bit

```

Maverick 64-bit:
```

wget http://mirrors.kernel.org/ubuntu/pool/main/g/grub2/grub-common_1.98+20100804-5ubuntu3_amd64.deb  # 64-bit grub-common
wget http://mirrors.kernel.org/ubuntu/pool/main/g/grub2/grub-pc_1.98+20100804-5ubuntu3_amd64.deb  # 64-bit grub-pc

```

Install grub-common first, then grub-pc, with:
dpkg -i <filename.deb>
You can use TAB-completion to complete the filename. (Example: type **dpkg -i grub-common  **then TAB to complete the name.

### Post by mozzart on 2010-10-15
i didnt use a Live CD, coz i'm running in a netbook. is there any solution ?

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-10-15
> **mozzart said:**
> i didnt use a Live CD, coz i'm running in a netbook. is there any solution ?

Are you at the grub rescue prompt?

I am working another thread in which I've asked the user if he/she can download an ISO and have it on an accessible partition. I haven't tried it from the rescue prompt, but I believe if we can get an image to a place where Grub2 can find it, we can manually type in the commands from the prompt to boot the LiveCD image. (If this works, I can see a new guide or an expansion of this one ...)

If you have the capability of downloading an ISO image, do this:
Go to [http://www.ubuntu.com/desktop/get-ubuntu/download]("http://www.ubuntu.com/desktop/get-ubuntu/download") and download the Ubuntu version you want (Lucid, Maverick, 32-bit, 64-bit, etc). 

Once downloaded, at the grub prompt try to find it. Use the "ls" command first to list your partitions. Once you find the /device/partition with your ISO image (for example, a USB drive), use this command to locate the image. Substitute the correct value for X,Y, and expand the path if the ISO is not located in /:
```
ls (hdX,Y)/

```
Example: ls (hd1,1)/

If you find it, post the location and the name of the ISO  you downloaded, and we'll see if we can come up with the commands to boot it from your netbook.

### Post by jamesamccarty on 2010-11-02
I followed the steps 2 through 5.  My Dell Inspiron 6000 still boots up to a mostly black screen with the "grub>" prompt.  I can get to the operating system by typing in the following:
linux /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-25-generic root=/dev/sda1 ro
initrd /boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-25-generic
boot

Everything works fine until I shut down, then I have to manually boot again.  I'm frustrated, but won't give up.  This all happened after a normal update a couple of weeks ago.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-02
> **jamesamccarty said:**
> I followed the steps 2 through 5.  My Dell Inspiron 6000 still boots up to a mostly black screen with the "grub>" prompt.  I can get to the operating system by typing in the following:
linux /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-25-generic root=/dev/sda1 ro
initrd /boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-25-generic
boot

Everything works fine until I shut down, then I have to manually boot again.  I'm frustrated, but won't give up.  This all happened after a normal update a couple of weeks ago.

If UUID's aren't working, which sounds like the change you have to make to get things working, you can change /etc/default/grub so UUID's are not used. Uncomment the following line, then update grub.
> 
GRUB_DISABLE_LINUX_UUID=true

If you still have problems, it could be the "search" line is causing difficulties. There are ways to eliminate that as well.

If the change to /etc/default/grub doesn't work, please let us know and post the contents of RESULTS.txt after running the boot info script from here:
[http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net]("http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net")

---

### Post by jamesamccarty on 2010-11-03
> **drs305 said:**
> If UUID's aren't working, which sounds like the change you have to make to get things working, you can change /etc/default/grub so UUID's are not used. Uncomment the following line, then update grub.


If you still have problems, it could be the "search" line is causing difficulties. There are ways to eliminate that as well.

If the change to /etc/default/grub doesn't work, please let us know and post the contents of RESULTS.txt after running the boot info script from here:
[http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net](http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net)

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-03
> **jamesamccarty said:**
> DRS305,

I uncommented the line that you suggested two ways.  One try was removing the # before the command.  No luck.  The other was to place quotations marks around true, as was suggested in the grub 2 documentation.  Still no luck.

Changing the line worked, as the UUID is no longer present in the "linux" line. 

The next thing to try is to remove the search line. While we can change the scripts to omit the "search" line, it would probably be easier for testing purposes to just edit grub.cfg once to see if removing the "search" line is the cause of your troubles.

Open /boot/grub/grub.cfg for editing (yes, I know it is not normally edited).
```
gksu gedit +154 /boot/grub/grub.cfg
```
This should open grub.cfg to the "search" line of your first menu entry (Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.32-25-generic). Remove the search line, save the file, *do **not** update grub*, reboot and select the first menu entry and see if it boots normally. If it boots normally, we will alter your scripts to eliminate the search line.

Added:
I also noted in your RESULTS.txt that you have a /grub/core.img file. I don't know how that folder/file ended up there, but it's not part of the grub file structure. The core.img file should be in /boot/grub and there is no /grub folder. You have copies of the correct folders/files, so you shouldn't move /grub/core.img to /boot/grub. I would either rename it or move it out of your system partition to prevent any confusion.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-04
> **jamesamccarty said:**

I am still assuming you can boot normally if you go to the grub prompt and type:
> linux /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-25-generic root=/dev/sda1 ro

Try editing grub.cfg again, make a backup, then remove the following section.

> [COLOR="Red"]
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,1)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 996f5576-b087-45d0-87ab-72b53b33dd06
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=640x480
  insmod gfxterm
  insmod vbe
  if terminal_output gfxterm ; then true ; else
    # For backward compatibility with versions of terminal.mod that don't
    # understand terminal_output
    terminal gfxterm
  fi
fi
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,1)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 996f5576-b087-45d0-87ab-72b53b33dd06
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale
set lang=en
insmod gettext[/COLOR]

Save the file, do NOT update grub, and see if it will boot from the first menu entry.

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-04
What result do you get when you run:
```
grub-editenv list

```
The */boot/grub/grubenv* file contains instructions to boot a preset menuentry.

At this point, once you boot to your normal installation (even if you have to type it) it might be time to just run "sudo grub-install /dev/sda" to rebuild the core.img (Note you do not use sda**1**)

Reboot and see what happens.

If you still have no success, (and you still have your backup of grub.cfg), edit your current shortened grub.cfg and remove *everything* above the line: 
> 
### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

Save the file and reboot. Press ENTER on the first entry. You will probably see a message about executing a command list, but it should boot.

If things still aren't working, I'd remake your grub.cfg file with:
```
sudo update-grub
```

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-05
@ jamesamccarty,

I think I know what is happening. Based on our discussions and trials, I went back and reviewed all the old posts. 

Since you system boots with only a minimal input into the terminal, the modules are being loaded and the paths are correct, I am pretty certain the missing link is grub.cfg. I don't believe G2 is seeing it.

The most logical reason for this is that your BIOS isn't looking beyond the 137GB limit. This was the case for older BIOS's, or perhaps a newer BIOS that doesn't have the disk options correctly set. 

Enter BIOS during boot and see what size it reports for your Ubuntu drive. If it doesn't list it as at least 200GB, see if there is a setting you can change. The reason for my suspicion is in the RESULTS.txt. Notice they are all below the 137GB limit *except* grub.cfg. 

> 
  98.9GB: boot/grub/core.img
 [COLOR="Red"]193.8GB[/COLOR]: boot/grub/grub.cfg
  99.0GB: boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-25-generic
  98.9GB: boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-25-generic
  99.0GB: initrd.img
  98.9GB: vmlinuz


The behavior you are getting is exactly the behavior G2 will exhibit if it can't find a valid grub.cfg file.

If you can't change the BIOS, try reinstalling grub and run the boot info script to see if G2 has now placed the grub.cfg file below the 137GB limit.
From your normal Ubuntu installation:
```

sudo grub-install --recheck /dev/sda
sudo update-grub
```

If it still doesn't find grub.cfg, the next time you get to the grub prompt, type:
```
ls (hd0,1)/boot/grub/grub.cfg
```
and see if it lists grub.cfg among the files.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-08
> **jamesamccarty said:**
> [COLOR=Blue]
I don't wish to have to back up everything and start over.  Is there any way to specify where on the hard drive to install a file?[/COLOR]


I was hoping I wouldn't have to address this because I'll have to show my ignorance on the manner. I don't know precisely how disk operations work. I spent some time this weekend playing with a system to find a solution, as I figured someone eventually would ask what you just did.

What I tried was to delete non-essential files on / . I deleted a lot of the /var/log backup files, ran "sudo apt-get clean", emptied root's trash, and then renamed the Grub2 file(s) outside the 137GB limit. I renamed the file(s) so the space they currently occupied couldn't be reused and the system would have to put the file(s) elsewhere. Then I ran the commands necessary to regenerate the files I'd renamed. In my case it was successful. So give these things a try, and then run "sudo update-grub" to generate a new grub.cfg and see where it is placed. 

Even if successful, there may be a time when files are once again put outside the limit. The long-term solution is to get a BIOS update or create a separate /boot partition. Hopefully someone can provide a better way around this issue.

### Post by drs305 on 2010-11-18
> **InTheMarket said:**
> Getting this for step 1:

ubuntu@ubuntu:~$ sudo mkdir /mnt/temp 
mkdir: cannot create directory `/mnt/temp': File exists # Cause I already did it.
ubuntu@ubuntu:~$ sudo mount /dev/sda1 /mnt/temp
mount: /dev/sda1 already mounted or /mnt/temp busy
mount: according to mtab, /dev/sda3 is already mounted on /mnt/temp #  Already did it.

Any help?

The only reason you would be mounting two partitions would be if you have a separate /boot partition. For a separate /boot, make sure it is mounted on /mnt/temp/boot

Try  starting over: unmount /dev/sda1, then unmount /dev/sda3. If you get an error message because it's busy, it means you have something else running that probably shouldn't be while you are accomplishing this procedure. While on the LiveCD, you should be able to unmount any normal partition.

### Post by COKEDUDE on 2010-12-02
Are there any advantages of using this grub as opposed to using this grub guide? 

[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1014708&highlight=grub](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1014708&highlight=grub)

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-02
> **COKEDUDE said:**
> Are there any advantages of using this grub as opposed to using this grub guide? 

[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1014708&highlight=grub](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1014708&highlight=grub)

That is an excellent link and written by *talsemgeest*, who was one of the first on these forums to recognize more documentation was needed regarding bootloading problems. The commands there are now widely repeated in posts throughout UF.

That thread concentrates on restoring the data in the MBR to point to the correct location of the boot files necessary to actually boot the OS. There are additional commands which detail how to fix Windows boot files which have become corrupt.

What it doesn't cover is how to repair or replace corrupted Grub files. The 'grub-install' commands writes to the MBR and creates a core.img file but it doesn't do much else. 

If a G2 file is corrupted reinstalling grub-common and grub-pc may replace the corrupt or missing files. However, if the user has intentionally or unintentionally manually deleted a Grub2 file, reinstalling will not replace it. (The thought being that the user has deleted this file so maybe there was a reason and G2 will defer to the user's action.)

To replace/restore a deleted file, the entire package (grub-common or grub-pc) must be removed (purged) and reinstalled. 

So in answer to your question, each thread has it's purpose. There are times when the user only needs to run the commands in *talsemgeest's*
 thread and there are others where more aggressive steps must be taken to restore Grub2.

---

**What it doesn't cover is how to repair or replace corrupted Grub files. The 'grub-install' commands writes to the MBR and creates a core.img file but it doesn't do much else. **

However, if the user has intentionally or unintentionally manually deleted a Grub2 file, reinstalling will not replace it. (The thought being that the user has deleted this file so maybe there was a reason and G2 will defer to the user's action.)

To replace/restore a deleted file, the entire package (grub-common or grub-pc) must be removed (purged) and reinstalled. 

So in answer to your question, each thread has it's purpose. There are times when the user only needs to run the commands in *talsemgeest's*
 thread and there are others where more aggressive steps must be taken to restore Grub2.

### Post by pgibson on 2010-12-05
> **drs305 said:**
> Ahhhh, are you saying I need to rewrite it to make it understandable?  ;-)

Haha, no, no, no!  In fact I think you have done quite a nice job writing a step-by-step that could even be followed by a willing-to-work-it-out beginner like me.  I can tell you gave some thought to who your audience might be: you define and clarify terms, something the common user always appreciates. I especially appreciated the places where you took the time to "telegraph" what was going to happen in the next step and what the reader could expect as a result.  For example:

> **3. Purge Grub 2 packages. The next command will remove grub, grub-pc (Grub 2) and grub-common.* Here is what you will do:**

    * Press ENTER to continue.
    * Read the warning during the install about removing the bootloader. TAB to highlight "<Yes>" and press ENTER.


This is a good example, too, of the way that you used bullet points to clarify within each enumerated step and idea.  It's an easier format in which to read and follow instructions than traditional paragraphed text blocks.    

Often, (and I can imagine this is not uncommon for beginners) I find myself only reading a HOWTO as part of an unexpected detour while I'm trying to do something else entirely; I'm flummoxed and prone to making decisions hastily because I'm in a hurry to get back to what I see as the task at hand.  

When I turn off the highway and I find myself lost, I want directions from a person who says something like, "Go straight down the road you're on.  The third or fourth intersection will be Main St, turn left on Main St.  Follow Main St. out of town one mile and you'll see signs for the highway."

Too often, trying to figure out what's wrong with my computer is like trying to get directions from a group of bickering locals 
[INDENT][I]
"Go down by old man Henderson's place, near the new sewer treatment plant ... " 

"Or he could go down to Overton Rd and go left at the quarry ..." 

"What?  That's crazy, look, don't listen to them ..." [/I][/INDENT] 

Your HOWTO is definitely in the former category.  My failures using it were primarily due to my own haste. When I slowed down enough to really read what was before me, I made it work.  I was a little thrown and distracted by color changes in text and a somewhat liberal placement of the asterix (such as in example three, above, where there is an asterix before the word "Here" and then two more used as bullets).  Although these little inconsistencies were distracting, making me work a bit harder to follow what you were saying, they are also mostly cosmetic and don't detract from the actual meaning of your writing.

You got me back to the highway, and THAT is what matters most! :D

[LEFT]PS As an aside. I read through hundreds of pages written by other people about GRUB. Whilst I applaud the desire to want to help us poor suckers, I really do, I do find it frustrating when people don't understand the barrier to progress that is thrown up by confusing terminology. People should read this guide and take note!! (I acknowledge it has been improved by helpful editors... and credit where it's due here also :) )
 
PPS Ok, so maybe I'll just drink a beer instead of the shrine thing. [/LEFT]

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  set have_grubenv=true
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ "${prev_saved_entry}" ]; then
  set saved_entry="${prev_saved_entry}"
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then
    saved_entry="${chosen}"
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n "${have_grubenv}" ]; then if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}

function load_video {
  insmod vbe
  insmod vga
}

insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,msdos6)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=640x480
  load_video
  insmod gfxterm
fi
terminal_output gfxterm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,msdos6)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale
set lang=en
insmod gettext
if [ "${recordfail}" = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.35-22-generic' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
    recordfail
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos6)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.35-22-generic root=UUID=613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d ro   quiet splash
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.35-22-generic
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.35-22-generic (recovery mode)' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
    recordfail
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos6)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d
    echo    'Loading Linux 2.6.35-22-generic ...'
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.35-22-generic root=UUID=613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d ro single 
    echo    'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.35-22-generic
}
### END /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###

    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos6)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d

}

    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos6)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 613fddcf-15ff-4878-9c22-bfd55bc84f3d

    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 152e87f73b46a78d
    chainloader +1
}

# menu entries you want to add after this comment.  Be careful not to change
# the 'exec tail' line above.
### END /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###

if [ -f  $prefix/custom.cfg ]; then
  source $prefix/custom.cfg;

# /etc/fstab: static file system information.

# for a device; this may be used with UUID= as a more robust way to name
# devices that works even if disks are added and removed. See fstab(5).

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-08
hemantkarandikar,

Welcome to the Ubuntu forums!

You appear to have the correct Grub2 files in the partition but your MBR still has Grub legacy (0,97). 

If your G2 files are indeed intact, all you should have to do is install it to the MBR. You can do this from the LiveCD (Lucid or Maverick) with the following commands:

```
sudo mount /dev/sda6 /mnt
sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sda
```
Do not use the partition number in the second command.

Reboot and then run "sudo update-grub" if it boots normally.

If it still doesn't boot, we will need to purge/reinstall Grub2. The guide for that is in my signature line, "G2-Chroot".

I reformatted your RESULTS.txt  When composing it, you can do the same by pasting the contents within [noparse]```
 
``` [/noparse] tags which you generate by pressing the **#** icon in the post's menubar.

---

### Post by ccrs8 on 2010-12-08
Hi.  When I follow the instructions to reinstall the grub packages (step 4), I get the part in 4a, but then instead of being given installation notes, I am immediately taken to a screen asking if I really don't want to install grub on any device.  I am never given an option to select a device.  In the background, the CLI is asking if I have mounted /dev.  I am able to see the boot drive and partition, and I mounted successfully in step 1.  Any suggestions?

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-09
> **ccrs8 said:**
> I am never given an option to select a device.  In the background, the CLI is asking if I have mounted /dev.  I am able to see the boot drive and partition, and I mounted successfully in step 1.  Any suggestions?

Only to reboot or unmount everything and try again. Did you get any error messages when running the "for i in ...." command. You can also try "ls /dev" to see if it exists and contains entries such as your partitions. 

It's easy to make a typo and I always recommend cut/pasting when possible.

---

### Post by ccrs8 on 2010-12-09
> **drs305 said:**
> Only to reboot or unmount everything and try again. Did you get any error messages when running the "for i in ...." command. You can also try "ls /dev" to see if it exists and contains entries such as your partitions. 

It's easy to make a typo and I always recommend cut/pasting when possible.

Heh...I know what I did.  When I read your code, I thought that the "for i in..." command was one of the commands that are only run if you have a separate boot partition.  So I did reboot and try again, but I skipped that command again, so naturally I got the same result.

Oh well.  I had my data backed up already and I have /home on a separate partition, so I just did a reinstall and I was back up and running soon enough.  A good punishment for not reading carefully enough, I think.

======================== sda3/Wubi/boot/grub/grub.cfg: ========================

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by /usr/sbin/grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ ${prev_saved_entry} ]; then
  set saved_entry=${prev_saved_entry}
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z ${boot_once} ]; then
    saved_entry=${chosen}
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n ${have_grubenv} ]; then if [ -z ${boot_once} ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}
insmod ntfs
set root='(hd0,3)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8490972c90972428
loopback loop0 /ubuntu/disks/root.disk
set root=(loop0)
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=640x480
  insmod gfxterm
  insmod vbe
  if terminal_output gfxterm ; then true ; else
    # For backward compatibility with versions of terminal.mod that don't
    # understand terminal_output
    terminal gfxterm
  fi
fi
insmod ntfs
set root='(hd0,3)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8490972c90972428
loopback loop0 /ubuntu/disks/root.disk
set root=(loop0)
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale
set lang=en
insmod gettext
if [ ${recordfail} = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
### END /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_lupin ###
menuentry "Ubuntu, Linux 2.6.32-26-generic" {
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(hd0,3)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8490972c90972428
    loopback loop0 /ubuntu/disks/root.disk
    set root=(loop0)
    linux /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-26-generic root=/dev/sda3 loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk ro   quiet splash
    initrd /boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-26-generic
}
menuentry "Ubuntu, Linux 2.6.32-26-generic (recovery mode)" {
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(hd0,3)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8490972c90972428
    loopback loop0 /ubuntu/disks/root.disk
    set root=(loop0)
    linux /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-26-generic root=/dev/sda3 loop=/ubuntu/disks/root.disk ro single 
    initrd /boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-26-generic
}
menuentry "Ubuntu, Linux 2.6.32-25-generic" {
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(hd0,3)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8490972c90972428
    loopback loop0 /ubuntu/disks/root.disk
    set root=(loop0)

    chainloader +1

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###
# This file provides an easy way to add custom menu entries.  Simply type the
# menu entries you want to add after this comment.  Be careful not to change
# the 'exec tail' line above.
### END /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###

============================= sda3/Wubi/etc/fstab: =============================

# /etc/fstab: static file system information.

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-13
> **Masterfod said:**
> 

at which point it told me this:
"chroot: cannot run command `/bin/bash': Exec format error"


I'll review the RESULTS.txt.

The most common reasons for the error are improperly mounting the necessary items (did you run the "for i in ..." command), or trying to use 32-bit CD with 64-bit installation (or vice versa).

If you don't know which version of the LiveCD you are running, use this command. If you get a colored result, you are using a 64-bit CD:
```
grep --color=always ' lm ' /proc/cpuinfo
```
If you have your real Ubuntu install mounted on /mnt/temp, run this to check the real installation. If it isn't mounted properly this may produce an error message or no result.
```
grep --color=always ' lm ' /mnt/temp/proc/cpuinfo
```

If you think you didn't run the mount commands correctly it would probably be best to restart the CD to begin anew.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-13
> **Masterfod said:**
> I ran those two commands, and here is the result:

Also, I think I did use a 64-bit installation, but I'm not sure. How would I check?

Edit: Just to note, the resulting text was grey, except for 'lm', which was red.

They are both 64-bit installs (red "lm" present) so that isn't the problem.

However, upon looking at RESULTS.txt, you are using a Wubi installation. There is a new Wubi Megathread by *Rubi1200* that should give you the information necessary to restore your system.
[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1639198]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1639198")


However #2, while you are on the LiveCD, if you don't currently get the Windows boot menu at startup, you can restore it with the following commands. This may be your only issue and may be all you need to do. Disregard the lilo messages - you don't want to run any additional commands:

```
sudo apt-get install lilo
sudo lilo -M /dev/sda mbr
```

This will restore the Windows bootloader.

### Post by YannBuntu on 2010-12-18
Dear drs305, thank you for your tutorial. :D

1) I saw many other similar tutorials that don't ask to mount /dev/pts (only the 3 other folders), do you know why ? is there a particular reason to mount /dev/pts ?

2) you recommend to use --recheck from a normal session, but not from a live-session , why ?  (in other words, why not recommending "sudo grub-install --recheck --root-directory=/mnt" from a live-session ?)

3) Does the --root-directory command work if the /boot is separate , or if it is a Wubi installation ?

best regards

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-18
YannBuntu,

1. Mounting */dev/pts* is probably not necessary for most users using the basic commands in this thread. The "pts" stands for "pseudo-terminal slave" and there are some programs that might require it's mounting. I believe *xterm* might be one of them. Since I refer users in other posts to use the 'chroot' procedures found in this guide to help solve their problems I tried to make the commands more universal in nature.

2, I don't include the "--recheck" in the 'chroot' section since we remove all traces of Grub during the purge of grub/grub-pc/grub-common. The original */boot/grub/device.map* would no longer exist. Any new one would reflect the existing conditions so there would be no need to check the contents.

3. The "--root-directory" switch does still work with a separate /boot partition. In fact, with a separate /boot partition it must be mounted for G2 to install properly. There are separate mounting instructions in the guide for users with systems containing a separate /boot partition.

I have not experimented with a separate /boot partition for Wubi and don't know if it's even possible. My opinion is that if you are to the point of wanting to create a separate /boot partition it's probably time to commit to an actual Ubuntu installation on a real partition.  ;-)

---

### Post by YannBuntu on 2010-12-18
Thank you for your answer.

1. "mount" is not recursive? I mean, mounting /dev does not mount also all its sub-directories (including */dev/pts*) ? 

2, I understand your explaination for the "purge" section, but I was talking about the "What can I try first?" section, which does not purge grub/grub-pc/grub-common. 

3. ok thank you

I agree with you about the separate /boot.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2010-12-18
> **YannBuntu said:**
> 1. "mount" is not recursive? I mean, mounting /dev does not mount also all its sub-directories (including */dev/pts*) ? 

If you run the "for i..." command with /dev/pts you will see various entries with "ls /mnt/temp/dev/pts". For me, it's "0 1 2 3 ptxm". 'ptxm' stands for 'pseudo-terminal master'

If you run the "for i ..." command with just /dev and run the same "ls" command it will show /mnt/dev/pts but it will be empty.

> 
2, I understand your explanation for the "purge" section, but I was talking about the "What can I try first?" section, which does not purge grub/grub-pc/grub-common. 
That's a good question. I have a reason, but I can't guarantee it's a good reason...  In a normal Grub2 installation, 'device.map' doesn't exist. It's something that the user would have to create, and it's not something that an inexperienced user would likely have (or know how to create). 

What is the downside of running "--recheck"? When you run "--recheck" G2 does check the existing 'device.map' file, but in my experiments it also *removes* the file. Since a user with the knowledge to create a device.map file probably had a reason for doing so, I didn't want to remove it; I decided someone with that expertise didn't need my help in restoring G2. I may be over-thinking this one and will have to spend some more time reconsidering on my decision.

Thanks for the questions and input.

---

Rebooting to Windows - is this a normal Ubuntu install or a Wubi install. If the latter, here is a good troubleshooting guide:
[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1639198]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1639198")

Regarding the umount command: If it's a normal installation and if you didn't get things mounted correctly the instructions may not have directed the results to the proper location. I'd try it again if you have the inclination.

If it was just a message about "/boot" not being mounted that is just because the command as I wrote it tries to unmount /boot if there was a separate partition.

### Post by mountney on 2011-01-14
Tried purging/re-installing Grub2 and still could not dual boot WinXP.

I had to create/[COLOR="Red"]modify[/COLOR] /etc/grub.d/11_Windows to boot WinXP...

#! /bin/sh -e
echo "Adding Windows XP" >&2
cat << EOF
menuentry "Windows XP" {
[COLOR="Red"]insmod ntfs[/COLOR]
set root=(hd1,1)
[COLOR="Red"]drivemap -s (hd0) \${root}[/COLOR]
chainloader +1
}
EOF

Then of course run update-grub and reboot...

Dell Optiplex GX520
2GB Ram
500GB Sata - Ubuntu 9.10 (/dev/sda)
250GB Sata - WinXP Professional (/dev/sdb)

Found that os-prober and thus 30_os-prober were NOT working so WinXP was not detected. Still researching why os-prober isn't detecting WinXP. I have three other systems, Debian 5 (64bit)/Win7 Ultimate (ASUS P6T), Ubuntu 10.04/WinXP (EPOX EP-4PLMI) and Ubuntu 9.10/WinXP (HP Pavilion dv8000 laptop) and on these os-prober/30_os-prober work fine.

Hope this helps some...

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-01-14
mountney,

One thing to check is that os-prober is actually installed (and executable):
```
ls /etc/grub.d/30-osprober
ls /usr/bin/os-prober
```
If it comes back empty it means it's not installed. 

Certain 3rd party Ubuntu installation disks, as well as Lubuntu, do not come with os-prober installed. The previous commands will check to see if the /etc/grub.d/30_-osprober script and file exists.

If it's not installed:
```
sudo apt-get install os-prober
```

### Post by drs305 on 2011-01-25
> **NeverSage said:**
> I know InTheMarket ended up getting this to work, but I'm having the same problem with no luck.  I've tried doing this again a couple times.  I have a separate boot partition on sda3 with everything else on sda1.  Do you have any more advice on what to try?  Thanks.

When I start getting 'busy' error messages I usually reboot and start over. Even using 'sudo' sometimes won't unmount everything.

Since you have a separate boot partition (sda3), *after* you mount /dev/sda1 to /mnt/temp, make sure you mount sda3 to /mnt/temp/boot. There are a few specific commands needed for users with a separate /boot partition, so make sure you also read and use those sections of the guide.

=========================== sda8/boot/grub/grub.cfg: ===========================

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  set have_grubenv=true
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ "${prev_saved_entry}" ]; then
  set saved_entry="${prev_saved_entry}"
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then
    saved_entry="${chosen}"
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n "${have_grubenv}" ]; then if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}

function load_video {
  insmod vbe
  insmod vga
}

insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,msdos8)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=640x480
  load_video
  insmod gfxterm
fi
terminal_output gfxterm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,msdos8)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale
set lang=en
insmod gettext
if [ "${recordfail}" = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.35-22-generic' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
    recordfail
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos8)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.35-22-generic root=UUID=8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20 ro   quiet splash
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.35-22-generic
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.35-22-generic (recovery mode)' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
    recordfail
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos8)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20
    echo    'Loading Linux 2.6.35-22-generic ...'
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.35-22-generic root=UUID=8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20 ro single 
    echo    'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.35-22-generic
}
### END /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###
menuentry "Memory test (memtest86+)" {
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos8)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20
    linux16    /boot/memtest86+.bin
}
menuentry "Memory test (memtest86+, serial console 115200)" {
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,msdos8)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 8dbcd3db-daa9-42b0-8ce5-9f88c6aa2c20
    linux16    /boot/memtest86+.bin console=ttyS0,115200n8
}
### END /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###
menuentry "Microsoft Windows XP Professional (on /dev/sda1)" {
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 3ea84680a8463725
    drivemap -s (hd0) ${root}
    chainloader +1
}
### END /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###
# This file provides an easy way to add custom menu entries.  Simply type the
# menu entries you want to add after this comment.  Be careful not to change
# the 'exec tail' line above.
### END /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/41_custom ###
if [ -f  $prefix/custom.cfg ]; then
  source $prefix/custom.cfg;
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/41_custom ###

=============================== sda8/etc/fstab: ===============================

# /etc/fstab: static file system information.

# Use 'blkid -o value -s UUID' to print the universally unique identifier
# for a device; this may be used with UUID= as a more robust way to name
# devices that works even if disks are added and removed. See fstab(5).

# <file system> <mount point>   <type>  <options>       <dump>  <pass>

# / was on /dev/sda8 during installation

/dev/sda6       none            swap    sw              0       0

This is correct.

> **yrelle said:**
> ubuntu@ubuntu:~$ for i in /dev /dev/pts /proc /sys; do sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i

> sudo cp /etc/resolv.conf /mnt/temp/etc/resolv.conf
> sudo chroot /mnt/temp
> ^C
ubuntu@ubuntu:~$ for i in /dev /dev/pts /proc /sys; do sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i[/CODE]

This is mistake. (missing "done" at the end, and this is redundant with the previous command).

I think you should restart your PC and try again...

### Post by drs305 on 2011-02-02
yrelle,

The instructions in this guide are for Grub2. You have Grub legacy installed on your system. You can upgrade to Grub2 using this guide. When you purge "grub-common" you will remove Grub legacy (grub). When you install grub-pc, you will be installing Grub2.

Since you have made a couple of typos attempting to run the commands, I strongly recommend you use cut/paste to transfer the commands to the terminal. CTRL-C to copy, CTRL-SHIFT-v to paste into terminal. Or if you use a mouse, highlight the command with the mouse to put it in memory, click in terminal and then middle click to paste.

---

### Post by goldcaddy77 on 2011-02-03
Hi everybody.  Long time windows user that just created an image of my windows 7 machine and wiped out my hard drive looking to run strictly Ubuntu.  I downloaded the Ubuntu 10.10 ISO, md5 checksum'd, burned on the lowest speed and installed, no problems.  When I rebooted, I got the same "Alert! /dev/disk/by-uuid/<<UUID>> does not exist. Dropping to a shell!" issue as most that have logged onto this thread have had.  I have tried purging and reinstalling grub2 from the live CD according to your steps twice now.  The instructions have notes 1, 2 and 3, then 2 code blocks:

```
### Run these two commands only if you have a separate /boot partition
sudo mkdir /mnt/boot
sudo mount /dev/sdXZ /mnt/boot  # Example: sudo mount /dev/sda6 /mnt/boot

```

```
sudo mount /dev/sdXY /mnt  # Example: sudo mount /dev/sda5 /mnt
sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sdX # Example: sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sda
```

...followed by the CHROOT steps.  I skipped the above steps and did the 1-8 CHROOT steps without any problems (including getting to the "root@ubuntu:/#" in terminal)  It was unclear whether or not I had to run the above code since it redirected me to the CHROOT steps.  Did I need to run the non-boot code above at some point or just the CHROOT steps?

When I reboot, I try to load from the hard disk normally and I still get the same (initramfas) "Gave up waiting... Dropping to a shell" error.

I have done the purge and reinstall grub2 twice now, so I think it's probably something else.

My bootinfo information can be found at the following link:

[http://paste.ubuntu.com/562346/](http://paste.ubuntu.com/562346/)

Note: I can only boot to the live CD if I enable to "acpi=off" setting.  If not, I get the following error: "(initramfs) Unable to find a medium containing a live file system" error.  If I set "acpi=off" I can boot from the live CD without a problem.  I'm not even sure what that setting does, but toggling it off allows me to boot to the CD.

I have been all over the forums looking for a similar scenario and I think this might be a first.  I really want to switch from Windows to Ubuntu, but after 2 days of struggling, I'm close to restoring my Win7 image.

Thanks in advance for your help.

Dan

Dan,

Welcome to the Ubuntu forums - sorry you are having difficulty with the installation. 

I happened upon your post just before retiring for the evening. 

No, you did not have to run the second set of commands, just the chroot steps. I'll take a look tomorrow and see if I can clarify things a bit.

If you had to add the "noacpi" option for the LiveCD, you may have to add it to the normal boot as well. Have you tried that?

If you don't know how:
During boot, if you see the Grub2 menu press "e" to edit the first menu entry. (If you don't see the Grub2 menu during boot, hold down the SHIFT key and see if it appears.)

Once you press "e" it should show the lines of the first menu entry. Use the cursors to navigate to the end of the line starting with "linux" and add this to the end, probably just after "quiet splash":  **acpi=off** (or whatever it is you typed for the LiveCD).

Once you have added the option to the end of the line, press "CTRL-x" to boot and see if the system now boots. If it does, you will have to add that option permanently to the Grub2 instructions. We'll tell you how to do it if that is what the problem is.

Your Grub2 file setup from RESULTS.txt appear pretty normal, but I have to admit I'm a bit tired. 

I will look at this again in the morning if work allows to see if "acpi" was the issue and restudy your boot info if your problem persists. 

We'd like to keep you on Ubuntu but realize we are off to a shaky start on your system.  ;-)

---

### Post by goldcaddy77 on 2011-02-04
Actually after digging around for the past 2 days, this was an easy one.  I modified file "/etc/default/grub"

```
sudo gedit /etc/default/grub
```

...and modified the line:

```
GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX=""
```

...to:

```
GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX="acpi=off"
```

This way, i'll be able to boot up both in normal mode and recovery, correct?

Again, thank you for your quick response.  I'm excited to get coding.

Dan

=========================== sda5/boot/grub/grub.cfg: ===========================

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by /usr/sbin/grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ ${prev_saved_entry} ]; then
  set saved_entry=${prev_saved_entry}
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z ${boot_once} ]; then
    saved_entry=${chosen}
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n ${have_grubenv} ]; then if [ -z ${boot_once} ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,5)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set f9f88d97-260d-496b-ab88-cd24a2960145
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=640x480
  insmod gfxterm
  insmod vbe
  if terminal_output gfxterm ; then true ; else
    # For backward compatibility with versions of terminal.mod that don't
    # understand terminal_output
    terminal gfxterm
  fi
fi
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,5)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set f9f88d97-260d-496b-ab88-cd24a2960145
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale
set lang=en
insmod gettext
if [ ${recordfail} = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

# Use 'blkid -o value -s UUID' to print the universally unique identifier

### Post by drs305 on 2011-02-25
> **davidtheduckfan said:**
> Everything looked find until step 4.**[COLOR=Navy]Reinstall the grub packages.[/COLOR]**

After adding the * in two of the three choices, I got an error saying something like couldn't add the grub to that partition. (don't know what I am talking about)

When you get to the step where you designate where Grub should write (Step 4), the only one you should select is "sda". Don't select "sda5" or sdb. Only one item should have an asterisk, then tab to OK and press ENTER. 

Of course, it's possible that drive could be identified as "sdb" or something else. In any case, you want to select the one that lists options for sdX and sdX5 - but only select sdX.

The contents of RESULTS.txt looks normal.

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-01
greenewbie,

What is booting is Grub legacy (Grub 0.97), as Grub2 doesn't provide notices such as those you quoted. This means that either Grub 2 is not installed or it is installed on the non-booting disk or partition. If you have two disks, you may need to change the boot order in BIOS if you think you may have installed it on a second disk.

We can see what is going on if you download and run the boot info script from the following site. Post the contents of the RESULTS.txt file it generates.

[http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net]("http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net")

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-02
greenewbie,

You should be able to switch the 'boot' drives via the BIOS setup. To enter BIOS, restart the machine. As it boots you have to press a key, usually one of the function keys (F1-F12), the DEL or ESC key. It may show on the startup screen which key to press to enter BIOS. If not, do a search for "BIOS key" and your computer brand and you will probably find out which key it is. At that point, you would go to the Boot page and select the other drive to boot first.

If you aren't seeing the Grub2 menu but it boots, it means that Grub2 hasn't found any other OS on your computer. If you also have Windows or another OS, after it boots, open a terminal and update Grub. It will conduct a search of your hard drives and if it finds Windows or another OS it will add it to the menu and the menu will now display for a period on boot to allow you to make a selection. To update Grub 2, run:
```
sudo update-grub
```

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-04
greenewbie,

Apologies for the delay in responding. I'm on vacation and, at best, only checking my subscribed threads once a day.

Grub 1.98 is Grub 2, even though it's a bit confusing. (Grub legacy, which has been around for years, never got past 0.97)...

Part of the Master/Slave setup depends on what type of cable you are using. It's best to break out the drive manuals and read through them, and refer to any pictures on the drives themselves. If at all possible use the "cable select" settings but of course it will depend on your specific drives. The following link gives a good picture of the 40 vs 80 wire cables. I think your problem will go away once you get the cables and jumpers set properly. You don't want both drives jumpered as the Master.


The SMART setting is a method by which the drive can be monitored to possibly detect impending hard drive failures. I've always left mine enabled and don't know if that can lead to any issues on some systems. If you search for "SMART hdd" you can get pretty good summaries of what the setting does.

 [http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html]("http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html")

This one has some good links to setting up the cables at the bottom:
[http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html]("http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html")


The other thing you can do is to point hdd1's MBR to your hdd2 Ubuntu setup. Boot to the normal Ubuntu desktop (without the LiveCD) and then run the following commands after you have both drives connected and working. You can check to make sure they are listed as sda and sdb with the "sudo fdisk -l" command (lowercase L). If they aren't sda and sdb, change the commands accordingly. This should put G2 on both MBRs pointing to your hdd2 Ubuntu partition:
```
sudo grub-install /dev/sda
sudo grub-install /dev/sdb
```

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-04
Cdocks,

Welcome to the Ubuntu forums!

Try booting the LiveCD and mounting your Ubuntu partition. Are you sure the Ubuntu partition is sda1? Do you have Windows also on the drive?

You can check the partitions with "sudo fdisk -l" (lowercase L). Your Ubuntu partition should show "Linux" under the last (System) column.

Once you are sure of the partition, run the following command. Open a terminal via Applications, Accessories, Terminal. If the partition is not sda1, change the command to the appropriate value. Don't mount sda1 and see if the following command will run:
```

sudo e2fsck -f -y -v /dev/sd**a1**
```
Hopefully you won't get the 'busy' message.

Also try to download and run the boot info script again. Read the instructions on the Boot Info Script page and follow the commands on how to run it.  If you have problems with it, give us the exact error messages that are generated when you try to run it. Being able to review the RESULTS.txt will be very helpful in analyzing your boot files, especially if the fsck/e2fsck command fixes any disk errors.

---

=========================== sda1/boot/grub/grub.cfg: ===========================

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by /usr/sbin/grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ ${prev_saved_entry} ]; then
  set saved_entry=${prev_saved_entry}
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z ${boot_once} ]; then
    saved_entry=${chosen}
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n ${have_grubenv} ]; then if [ -z ${boot_once} ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,1)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 75fc7b82-e884-4d0d-80ac-48bac539236c
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=640x480
  insmod gfxterm
  insmod vbe
  if terminal_output gfxterm ; then true ; else
    # For backward compatibility with versions of terminal.mod that don't
    # understand terminal_output
    terminal gfxterm
  fi
fi
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,1)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 75fc7b82-e884-4d0d-80ac-48bac539236c
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale
set lang=en
insmod gettext
if [ ${recordfail} = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.32-28-generic' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
    recordfail
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,1)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 75fc7b82-e884-4d0d-80ac-48bac539236c
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-28-generic root=UUID=75fc7b82-e884-4d0d-80ac-48bac539236c ro   quiet splash
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-28-generic
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.32-28-generic (recovery mode)' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
    recordfail
    insmod ext2
    set root='(hd0,1)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set 75fc7b82-e884-4d0d-80ac-48bac539236c
    echo    'Loading Linux 2.6.32-28-generic ...'
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.32-28-generic root=UUID=75fc7b82-e884-4d0d-80ac-48bac539236c ro single 
    echo    'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.32-28-generic
}

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###
if [ ${timeout} != -1 ]; then
  if keystatus; then
    if keystatus --shift; then
      set timeout=-1
    else
      set timeout=0
    fi
  else
    if sleep --interruptible 3 ; then
      set timeout=0
    fi
  fi
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-09
> **Cdocks said:**
> 
Should I still Purge and reinstall Grub? 

First, congratulations on clearing the disk error messages.  :-)
Have you tried booting it without the LiveCD? If so, what happens? 

The contents of RESULTS.txt appears normal. If it doesn't boot and you end up at a grub rescue or grub prompt, or get a Grub error message, you could try purging and reinstalling G2. This is what I recommend when things appear normal but the system still doesn't boot and it's a Grub2 failure.

If you end up at a blinking cursor or get to the Ubuntu logo or further, it isn't a G2 issue.

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-14
> **Engineeringtech said:**
> 
First of all, how DO you "chroot"?  You never really explain it.  Do I have to tell the command where the new root is?
The procedure is laid out in the first post. Each step in the "Chroot" section is required for the procedure to run. Step 1 is the section in which the actual 'chroot' occurs.

> 
Secondly, must I have an internet connection to remove Grub Legacy and Grub2? Or install Grub2?

You don't need an internet connection to uninstall Grub2. But you would normally need a connection to install grub legacy.

Disclaimer: I cannot guarantee this will work, as I haven't tried it, but I will give you the basic outline. There will be more info you will need to know if you want to continue. Just ask. ***[COLOR="DarkRed"]Do not purge any files at this time as this post provides a general overview but not the specific details.[/COLOR]***

Without an internet connection you will not be able to download the necessary packages. You might be able to download the grub legacy package (grub_097-29ubuntu60_amd64.deb or grub_097-29ubuntu60_i386.deb) from another computer and put it on a flash drive. You could then install it if your computer has the required dependencies already installed. According to my version of 10.10, the required dependencies for "grub" are:
[I]grub-common 
udev (>= 117-5)
ucf (>= 3.004-0ubuntu2)
debconf (>= 1.5.19)
util-linux (>= 2.15-1)[/I]
You can see if they are installed by looking in Synaptic if you can run your Ubuntu OS, or you can mount the partition and explore the /var/lib/dpkg/status file to see if the packages are installed and of sufficient recency.

If they are and you can get a copy of "grub.deb", you may be able to install it with the dpkg command. I'll give that to you later if you really decide to pursue this.

> 
I have my XT4 root partition at /dev/sda9, and swap partition at /dev/sda10.  I want Grub Legacy installed PROPERLY on the Ubuntu root partition, and NOT the MBR.  What specific commands would I run from a terminal window to unistall Grub Legacy, and Grub2, then REINSTALL Grub Legacy to the root ?

I would have to refer you to old posts on how to set up Grub legacy to the MBR. I don't know if you can install it directly to a partition when you install Grub or if you would allow a normal installation and then move it to a partition. There are threads on this but I've not used Grub legacy in a long time.

> 
P.S. One more thing....I have tried using SuperGrub to verify that my installation is bootable.  That too has failed repeatedly.  I always get a message that FSTAB is missing..  OldFred thinks there is something wrong with my system that I am not getting a full installation.  But I never get any error messages from the installer whether I installed Grub2 to the MBR, or not.  I even tried to install Fedora 14 and got the same failure.

*oldfred* is someone to listen to. He provides outstanding advice and knows his way around both Ubuntu and Windows. I'm pretty much worthless with Windows as I've been away from it for too long.

If you are having a problem with fstab that is not likely to be a grub problem, and if SuperGrub also can't boot it would further the chances of some other problem.

> 
Please don't ask me to justify in detail why I want to manually install Grub Legacy

I normally may provide pros and cons but generally will provide the advice requested as long as it conforms to the forum Code of Conduct and I have the time.

IMO, as a relatively inexperienced Linux user I think you are probably trying to do things that will end up frustrating you. But how much time and energy you want to try to spend on this is up to you.

If you still want to remove Grub2 and install Grub legacy, you will need to download a copy of the grub deb after you ensure you already have the necessary dependencies. If the dependencies are not aleady installed, you could get into 'dependency hell' trying to find all the needed packages.

If you want to continue, have obtained a copy of the grub*.deb and have the required dependencies, let me know.

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-16
First, although I don't know if this is causing problems, is to get rid of any residual RAID bits. Grub2 and RAID sometimes get along. I don't use RAID so I can't give advice on installing it, but to remove the remnants of a previous RAID setup you can run the following. Run the appropriate commands for whichever drive (sda, sdb, etc) you are trying to work with:
```

sudo dmraid -E -r /dev/sda
sudo dmraid -E -r /dev/sdb
sudo apt-get remove dmraid

```

Chainloading is possible with Grub2. The problem with putting G2 on a partition is that if the files move Grub2 will break.

I really don't know why fstab and menu.lst are missing. I guess I'd first boot to any OS and do a search for the files to confirm they are really missing. 

If they are found (especially Grub files) once you've booted an OS it's possible the BIOS doesn't see that part of the disk. Both fstab and menu.lst could have been rewritten and possibly placed near the end of the disk. I can envision cases where the BIOS couldn't find menu.lst before the system can view the entire partition.

A BIOS limitation would explain why you don't get error messages. An OS would see the entire disk and think (correctly) that the installation was completed without errors. But if BIOS can't find the files it is looking for the OS will not boot. 

That's the only thing I can think of, but if your motherboard was still under warranty I would think the BIOS shouldn't be a limitation.

As for 9.10, it's 'End of Life' is next month, so I don't think that is a viable alternative. It will still run, but there will be no support and you'll miss out on a lot of advances since it was designed.

---
