### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-17
> **Engineeringtech said:**
> I was hoping as a last ditch I could install 9.04 instead of 10.10.  But you're telling me even 9.10 is going off support.  So I guess it's time for me to give up on this.  Maybe God's not going to let me have this computer.


Sometimes it's hard to believe some plastic and silicon can cause so much grief. ](*,)

I'd probably give up and relent to having one OS on the machine, but given the circumstances if you thought it would work you could install 9.04. The OS won't stop working, you just won't get updates. The official repositories will be pulled down but will be available from [http://old-releases.ubuntu.com/releases/](http://old-releases.ubuntu.com/releases/) so you will still be able to add/remove the packages that existed at the time the repositories were closed.

Good luck with whatever you decide to do with this computer.

1. Chroot into your real system. FASE A.

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-21
jadeye,

I don't know if your Ubuntu installs are recoverable. You are missing key files (initrd.img) and Grub 2 isn't properly installed. I am not a Windows expert but let's try a simple way to restore Windows at least.

Boot the Ubuntu LiveCD. We will try to make the sdb MBR point to the Windows bootloader files in sdb1.

First, make sure sdb is your Windows drive in the results from the command:
```
sudo fdisk -l  # Lowercase L
```
The results should show the Windows drive as your sdb drive as below:
> Disk /dev/sd**b**: 122.9 GB, 122942324736 bytes
Next run these two commands. They will point the sdb MBR to Windows. Do not run additional lilo commands. The two are all we need:
```
sudo apt-get install lilo
sudo lilo -M /dev/sdb mbr
```

Reboot and enter BIOS setup. You will to press a key during boot. It's usually one of the function keys (F1-F12), the DEL key or ESC key. 

In BIOS, change the boot order so sdb (the 122GB drive) boots first.

Hopefully that will allow Windows to boot. Let us know what happens.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-25
jadeye,

In the RESULTS.txt, it says that sda1 is a development (beta) version of Maverick. Is that what you are trying to recover? Or did you have an Ubuntu installation within Windows (WUBI) that you are trying to recover?

You are still missing the initrd files, so I would try to just recover your data from the Ubuntu install and then reinstall the OS.

Boot the LiveCD:
If you had a normal Ubuntu installation on sda1, you can mount that partition and then use nautilus (file browser) to find the folders you want to save and move/copy them elsewhere:
```

sudo mount /dev/sda1 /mnt
gksu nautilus /mnt
```

If you had a WUBI install, your files are stored in a file called 'root.disk', which should be located in your Windows partition at /ubuntu/disks/root.disk.

To access it, mount the Windows partition and look for root.disk. If you find it, then you mount that file as well.

```
sudo mkdir /mnt/windows
sudo mount /dev/sdb1 /mnt/windows
gksu nautilus /mnt/windows
```
If you find root.disk, mount it. In the following command, I'm using its normal location. If you find it elsewhere, change the command to point to the real location.
```
sudo mount -o loop /mnt/windows/ubuntu/disks/root.disk /mnt/wubi
```

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-25
> **jadeye said:**

Unless the file is elsewhere on the partition I don't know of a way to restore it. You can reinstall wubi to create a new one but it's not going to be able to recover your old files.

>

The ... part of the initrd.img could be important. If it's initrd.img-2.6.35-20-generic then you might be able to copy it to your /boot folder in sda1.

In fact, look in the /media folder you referenced and see if you have an entire /boot folder. You don't have a grub.cfg menu either in sda1, according to RESULTS.txt. You might be able to get something to boot if you can find the entire /boot folder and subfolders in /media and try copying them back to sda1's /boot folder.

All this is a pretty long shot, and the option most likely to recover your data files is just to mount /dev/sda1, look in your /home folder and save what files you can.

### Post by empitt on 2011-03-25
The script Ubumatic allows easy reinstallation of Grub 2 from Live CD. I prepared an English version a few days ago: [http://www.linuxone.pl/ubumaticen](http://www.linuxone.pl/ubumaticen). Run the script:
```
sudo ./ubumatic.sh --restoregrub
```

### Post by drs305 on 2011-03-26
jadeye,

You can't reconfigure G2 from the LiveCD without using chroot since the commands try to change the CD files, which can't be done. By chrooting into your real installation, the commands then will affect your hard drive files.

 If you are going to go to the trouble of chrooting from the CD, I'd recommend a complete purge and reinstall of Grub2. The details are outlined in the first post of this thread.

If you want to try to restore your Maverick OS, the partition to mount is /dev/sda1.

Normally this is the result of the target partition not being mounted first. 

Before running the "for i in ...." command the Ubuntu partition must be mounted (to /mnt/temp in this post's commands).

Make sure the OS partition is correctly mounted to /mnt/temp. You can check with "ls /mnt/temp" and it should return the normal Ubuntu folders such as /bin /boot /home, ...). 

And I always recommend copy/pasting the commands when possible to prevent typos.

### Post by drs305 on 2011-04-01
YannBuntu,

I appreciate your work on this matter.

My one comment on the purge issue. Reinstalling Grub2 will solve some problems but will not completely remove and *replace* all the Grub 2 files. 

In instances where a Grub file becomes corrupted, or where a user removes a file (intentionally or not) the installation will not replace the file and the boot will continue to fail.

Purging the files provides a completely 'fresh' start with Grub 2 and is normally the solution when lesser attempts fail.

However, I understand that users' wish lists must be subject to the time available to *volunteers* and there are undoubtedly higher priorities.

Thanks again.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-04-02
debd,

When running a normal OS, your boot and home parition contents are mounted to their correct location according to the instructions in fstab. Since they are mounted during the boot, you always see them inside their mount points (/boot and /home).

When you look at them from the LiveCD, those partitions haven't been mounted and /boot and /home in your Ubuntu partition would be empty (more on that in a bit). There have been no instructions to mount the contents of the boot and home partitions to /boot and /home. That is why you must mount them manually during the 'chroot' procedure.

Since you said you can see the grub folder/contents in /boot, what has probably happened is that at some point there was an error in either moving or copying the /boot contents. 

This normally happens when something is copied to a mount point before a partition was mounted there. The /boot folder exists on Ubuntu, whether or not your boot partition is mounted or not. If the boot partition is mounted on /boot, anything you copy to /boot is actually copied to your boot partition. If you could unmount the boot partition, the copied files would not be visible on the OS partition - they would be on the unmounted partition.

If the boot partition isn't yet mounted, anything you copy to /boot is written to the OS partition. When you mount your boot partition to /boot, anything currently on /boot is hidden underneath the contents of your boot partition and remain hidden and unavailable until you unmount your boot partition.

Technically, you could remove any contents in /boot from the LiveCD as long as your boot partition wasn't yet mounted. The reason you would have to use the LiveCd or from another running OS is that you cannot unmount /boot while running that OS, so the additional files can't be seen or removed at that time.

Since your system is working and the /boot contents normally aren't very large, I'd leave things as they are. 

I know it's confusing, and if I could give you a good reference to explain it I would. Someone else might be able to provide one that can demonstrate is a lot better than I can.

I'm a big fan of this booklet as an introduction to Ubuntu, but I don't know if/how it addresses this issue.
[Ubuntu Pocket Guide]("http://www.ubuntupocketguide.com/download_main.html")

---

### Post by greenewbie on 2011-04-06
> **drs305 said:**
> greenewbie,

Apologies for the delay in responding. I'm on vacation and, at best, only checking my subscribed threads once a day.

Grub 1.98 is Grub 2, even though it's a bit confusing. (Grub legacy, which has been around for years, never got past 0.97)...

Part of the Master/Slave setup depends on what type of cable you are using. It's best to break out the drive manuals and read through them, and refer to any pictures on the drives themselves. If at all possible use the "cable select" settings but of course it will depend on your specific drives. The following link gives a good picture of the 40 vs 80 wire cables. I think your problem will go away once you get the cables and jumpers set properly. You don't want both drives jumpered as the Master.


The SMART setting is a method by which the drive can be monitored to possibly detect impending hard drive failures. I've always left mine enabled and don't know if that can lead to any issues on some systems. If you search for "SMART hdd" you can get pretty good summaries of what the setting does.

 [http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html](http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html)

This one has some good links to setting up the cables at the bottom:
[http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html](http://www.onlinecomputertips.com/hardware/hd_jumpers.html)


The other thing you can do is to point hdd1's MBR to your hdd2 Ubuntu setup. Boot to the normal Ubuntu desktop (without the LiveCD) and then run the following commands after you have both drives connected and working. You can check to make sure they are listed as sda and sdb with the "sudo fdisk -l" command (lowercase L). If they aren't sda and sdb, change the commands accordingly. This should put G2 on both MBRs pointing to your hdd2 Ubuntu partition:
```
sudo grub-install /dev/sda
sudo grub-install /dev/sdb
```

### Post by drs305 on 2011-04-07
@ greenewbie,

I'm in a hotel with really intermittent Internet. It seems there are issues me getting on UF this morning as well so this could be cut a bit short. I'll try to address your issues.

First, I'd recover your Firefox settings (including your profile and most likely your FF passwords). If you can mount your old partition via the LiveCD, copy the /home/your.username/.mozilla/firefox folder somewhere. The folder you really want will be the alphanumeric.default folder in the firefox subfolder. When you reinstall, you can choose a new profile, point it to this folder on your new system (you can copy it to /home/your.username/.mozilla/firefox/... ) and it should have all your old settings.

It doesn't look like your Ubuntu install is available, since the script didn't find anything. But you could run an fsck check on it to see if that fixes things. Again from the LiveCD, mount your Ubuntu partition (I'll assume sda1), then run the check:
```
sudo mount /dev/sda1 /mnt
sudo e2fsck -f -y -v /dev/sda1
```

Note: The 'disks nor in order' message with fdisk is not a big deal. It can be fixed but it doesn't really hurt the operation of the system.

If you wanted to purge and reinstall grub2, you would have to chroot into your old Ubuntu partition using the procedures in my 'chroot' link in the signature line. I wouldn't bother unless the fsck check now allows normal boot info script results (which show files in your Ubuntu partition).

So, unless the fsck check fixes the Ubuntu partition, a new installation is probably in order. Save whatever folders you want to back up.

As far as your attempts at 'grub-install', the command in your situation (LiveCd) requires a location to write the files followed by a drive on which to alter the MBR. It wouldn't work without the target since it would try to write to the CD. So the command would have been, after mounting your Ubuntu partition on /mnt:  sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sda

One final thing: I edited your post to enclose large terminal output in 'code' tags. You do that by putting the content within [noparse] ```
 
``` [/noparse] tags. You can press the # icon in the post's menu to auto-generate the tags.

I probably won't be able to review this thread until tomorrow should you have any questions.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-04-09
greenewbie,

I don't think you are having a grub problem. Your system is having a problem reading the partitions. There is corruption of some sort, and the files aren't being read. It could be the filesystem, partition table, or something else. 

The mount command normally wouldn't require you to add the file type for a linux partition, which just further confirms the problem (unless sda1 isn't a linux partition and you are trying to mount a non-linux partition).

I am not an expert on recovery issues. Since your post is in the Grub2 thread there's a good chance that those who are will not see it.

You might start a new thread in Absolute Beginners or General and explain what's happening. You might get help that will restore your current setup. Best of luck either way.

### Post by empitt on 2011-04-26
> **greenewbie said:**
> This didn't work for me either, I guess it's not relevant if I only have Grub 0.97 installed. My terminal said:

[COLOR=Red]sudo: ./ubumatic.sh: command not found[/COLOR]

You read on page [www.linuxone.pl/ubumaticen]("http://www.linuxone.pl/ubumaticen") (new address). In the console enter the command:
```
cd ~ && wget http://www.linuxone.pl/download/ubumatic_en.tar.gz && tar xzf ubumatic_en.tar.gz && sudo ./ubumatic.sh --restoregrub
```

============================= sda2/grub/grub.cfg: =============================

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  set have_grubenv=true
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ "${prev_saved_entry}" ]; then
  set saved_entry="${prev_saved_entry}"
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then
    saved_entry="${chosen}"
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n "${have_grubenv}" ]; then if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}

function load_video {
  insmod vbe
  insmod vga
  insmod video_bochs
  insmod video_cirrus
}

insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(/dev/sda,msdos5)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root fcc5090e-8a90-4102-9ec7-f20a60f1e7db
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=auto
  load_video
  insmod gfxterm
fi
terminal_output gfxterm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ntfs
set root='(/dev/sda,msdos2)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 02A495B6A495AD23
set locale_dir=($root)/grub/locale
set lang=en_US
insmod gettext
if [ "${recordfail}" = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
if background_color 44,0,30; then
  clear
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
if [ ${recordfail} != 1 ]; then
  if [ -e ${prefix}/gfxblacklist.txt ]; then
    if hwmatch ${prefix}/gfxblacklist.txt 3; then
      if [ ${match} = 0 ]; then
        set linux_gfx_mode=keep
      else
        set linux_gfx_mode=text
      fi
    else
      set linux_gfx_mode=text
    fi
  else
    set linux_gfx_mode=keep
  fi
else
  set linux_gfx_mode=text
fi
export linux_gfx_mode
if [ "$linux_gfx_mode" != "text" ]; then load_video; fi
### END /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###
menuentry "Windows Recovery Environment (loader) (on /dev/sda1)" --class windows --class os {
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(/dev/sda,msdos1)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 427094D47094CFD1
    drivemap -s (hd0) ${root}
    chainloader +1
}
menuentry "Windows 7 (loader) (on /dev/sda2)" --class windows --class os {
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ntfs
    set root='(/dev/sda,msdos2)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 02A495B6A495AD23
    chainloader +1
}
### END /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###
# This file provides an easy way to add custom menu entries.  Simply type the
# menu entries you want to add after this comment.  Be careful not to change
# the 'exec tail' line above.
### END /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/41_custom ###
if [ -f  $prefix/custom.cfg ]; then
  source $prefix/custom.cfg;
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/41_custom ###

=================== sda2: Location of files loaded by Grub: ===================


    ??GB: grub/core.img
    ??GB: grub/grub.cfg

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  set have_grubenv=true
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ "${prev_saved_entry}" ]; then
  set saved_entry="${prev_saved_entry}"
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then
    saved_entry="${chosen}"
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n "${have_grubenv}" ]; then if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}

function load_video {
  insmod vbe
  insmod vga
  insmod video_bochs
  insmod video_cirrus
}

insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(/dev/sda,msdos5)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root fcc5090e-8a90-4102-9ec7-f20a60f1e7db
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=auto
  load_video
  insmod gfxterm
fi
terminal_output gfxterm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(/dev/sda,msdos5)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root fcc5090e-8a90-4102-9ec7-f20a60f1e7db
set locale_dir=($root)/boot/grub/locale

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-02
> **TimothyLucas said:**
> And just to be a little helpful; I've also ran the boot_info_script.sh, here are it's results,

There is some strange output in the RESULTS.txt saying sda5 is NTFS, but everything else points to it being ext4 and your Ubuntu installation, so that is what I'm going to assume.

You have Grub files installed on a Windows partition (sda2), which isn't where they should be. The initial line of RESULTS.txt doesn't tell us exactly where the Grub files are installed, but they may not be on sda5. That's where they need to be, so the following will make sure Grub points the MBR to sda5.

From the LiveCD, mount your Ubuntu partition. Just to be sure, the second command will list the contents of your /boot partition. Make sure the vmlinuz... and initrd... files are there, then continue.

The following is if you are using a Natty LiveCD (Grub 1.99):
```

sudo mount /dev/sda5 /mnt
ls /mnt/boot  # Check for files.
sudo grub-install --boot-directory=/mnt/boot /dev/sda
```
If you are using a Maverick or earlier CD, or have Grub 1.98, use this as your last command rather than the one above:
```
sudo grub-install --root-directory=/mnt /dev/sda
```
Don't put the partition number in the last command.

Reboot.

---

### Post by TimothyLucas on 2011-05-03
Okay, that worked, so the solution was to remove all data in my first 63 sectors of the harddisk (so it wipes clean FlexNet) and then to reinstall grub. FlexNet is either:
a) Something to do with MATLAB
b) Something to do with Windows (which I'm going to throw off anyway)

Thank you for all the help!

        (In some cases useful info about processes that use
         the device is found by lsof(8) or fuser(1))

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-03
> **TimothyLucas said:**
> Okay, that worked, so the solution was to remove all data in my first 63 sectors of the harddisk (so it wipes clean FlexNet) and then to reinstall grub. FlexNet is either:

b) Something to do with Windows (which I'm going to throw off anyway)


Flexnet is associated with Windows, specifically Adobe. The Grub devs have worked on fixes so Grub doesn't have to write to that portion of the disk but there are lots of Internet threads about Flexnet, Dell DataSafe and HP ProtectTools screwing up the MBR.

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-03
> **rookie127 said:**
> 
after i press tab i get al the list of command 
so what should i do

Probably the best thing to do is boot the LiveCD and download/run the boot info script from the following site. 
[http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net]("http://bootinfoscript.sourceforge.net")
Open a new post, then press the # icon in the post's menu bar. This will generate 'code' tags. Copy the contents of the RESULTS.txt file between them.

---

============================= sda1/grub/grub.cfg: =============================

#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  set have_grubenv=true
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ "${prev_saved_entry}" ]; then
  set saved_entry="${prev_saved_entry}"
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then
    saved_entry="${chosen}"
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n "${have_grubenv}" ]; then if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}

function load_video {
  insmod vbe
  insmod vga
  insmod video_bochs
  insmod video_cirrus
}

insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(/dev/sda,msdos2)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 109ed6a4-2a6f-466b-a54b-10edaf8bcdea
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=auto
  load_video
  insmod gfxterm
fi
terminal_output gfxterm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ntfs
set root='(/dev/sda,msdos1)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root CAD4B0BBD4B0AB59
set locale_dir=($root)/grub/locale
set lang=en_US
insmod gettext
if [ "${recordfail}" = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
if background_color 44,0,30; then
  clear
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
if [ ${recordfail} != 1 ]; then
  if [ -e ${prefix}/gfxblacklist.txt ]; then
    if hwmatch ${prefix}/gfxblacklist.txt 3; then
      if [ ${match} = 0 ]; then
        set linux_gfx_mode=keep
      else
        set linux_gfx_mode=text
      fi
    else
      set linux_gfx_mode=text
    fi
  else
    set linux_gfx_mode=keep
  fi
else
  set linux_gfx_mode=text
fi
export linux_gfx_mode
if [ "$linux_gfx_mode" != "text" ]; then load_video; fi
### END /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###
if [ "x${timeout}" != "x-1" ]; then
  if keystatus; then
    if keystatus --shift; then
      set timeout=-1
    else
      set timeout=0
    fi
  else
    if sleep --interruptible 3 ; then
      set timeout=0
    fi
  fi
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###

    recordfail
    set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
    insmod part_msdos
    insmod ext2
    set root='(/dev/sda,msdos2)'
    search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 109ed6a4-2a6f-466b-a54b-10edaf8bcdea
    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.38-8-generic root=UUID=109ed6a4-2a6f-466b-a54b-10edaf8bcdea ro   quiet splash vt.handoff=7
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.38-8-generic
}

    linux    /boot/vmlinuz-2.6.38-8-generic root=UUID=109ed6a4-2a6f-466b-a54b-10edaf8bcdea ro single 
    echo    'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
    initrd    /boot/initrd.img-2.6.38-8-generic
}

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-03
rookie127,

You have Grub files installed on several partitions. Let's start by trying to put Grub in the MBR and pointing to sda2, your Ubuntu partition:

Boot the LiveCD and open a terminal, then run:
```
sudo mount /dev/sda2 /mnt
sudo grub-install --boot-directory=/mnt/boot /dev/sda
```

Do not include the partition number in the second command.

Reboot. If successful, then run:
```
sudo update-grub
```
which will hopefully find your Windows installation.

Your Windows partitions also contain a *grub* folder. If and when you boot Windows, you should remove the *grub* folder from sda1 and sda3.

---

### Post by kansasnoob on 2011-05-07
I double-checked my commands there this AM and I think it's good to go. I also added a bit about collecting hardware info:

> I'm wondering if it's hardware related so I think it would be very helpful to collect a hardware profile by running:

sudo lshw > hardwareprofile.txt

You'll then find a file in your home folder titled "hardwareprofile.txt", so if you'll go to that bug report, make a brief comment, and click on "Add attachment or patch" a new window will open and clicking on "Browse" will allow you to locate that file.

There may be a more eloquent way of doing that but from toying around that seems to show the best MoBo and BIOS info :)

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-09
@ kansasnoob,

I have a theory on the failures.

If a user installs Natty but fails to designate the drive on which to install grub the old grub information remains in part on the system. Since 1.98 and 1.99 are so different, Grub can't handle it and the boot fails.

How it can happen: I can visualize three scenarios. 

1. During upgrade the user fails to select a drive on which to install grub. This can happen if the user presses ENTER without first pressing the SPACEBAR to select a drive. Note the user is then given a message that if he/she continues no NEW information is written to the MBR. I worked with a user who had done this yesterday.

2. Trying to fix grub, the user mixes CDs/OSs and uses a Natty CD with a Lucid/Mav OS, or a Lucid/Mav OS with a Natty CD. Just running grub-install without completely purging/reinstalling will result in a mixed grub install and will fail.

It would be very likely that someone doing an online upgrade to Natty would not have a Natty CD, and would use a Lucid/Maverick CD to try to repair G2.

3. The user updates the Grub files and MBR properly but uses a different boot drive. I am not sure this scenario will fail if the boot MBR uses a different Grub version (1.98/1.99) but it might.

---

### Post by benny777 on 2011-05-25
I have got up to the part of accessing the "i in.." information. i type in.
sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i 
and get
mount: can't find /mnt/temp in /etc/fstab or /etc/mtab

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-25
> **benny777 said:**
> I have got up to the part of accessing the "i in.." information. i type in.
sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i 
and get
mount: can't find /mnt/temp in /etc/fstab or /etc/mtab

Have you created the /mnt/temp files in Step 1? Once you have done that the next command is all one line:
```
for i in /dev /dev/pts /proc /sys; do sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i;  done
```

### Post by YannBuntu on 2011-05-26
Dear all,

I have a good news : the "Boot-Repair" graphical tool now proposes an option to purge and reinstall GRUB2 !!!

Enjoy :D

[[img]http://pix.toile-libre.org/upload/img/1299426403.png[/img]](http://pix.toile-libre.org/?img=1299426403.png)
[[img]http://pix.toile-libre.org/upload/img/1306401412.png[/img]](http://pix.toile-libre.org/?img=1306401412.png)

**Download/Install Boot-Repair in Ubuntu :**
either add ‘ppa:yannubuntu/boot-repair’ to your Software Sources via the Software Centre or, for speeds-sake, add it using a new Terminal session: 

[B]*   sudo add-apt-repository ppa:yannubuntu/boot-repair
*   sudo apt-get update && sudo apt-get install boot-repair-ubuntu[/B]


Boot-repair can be installed and used either from a live-CD, a live-USB, or a Ubuntu session installed on disk.

PPA packages are available for Ubuntu 10.04, 10.10 and 11.04.

**Use Boot-repair:**
Launch it from System->Administration->Boot-Repair menu if you use Gnome, or search "boot" in the dash if you use Unity. Then follow the menus...

**You can contribute by** :
- [translating]("http://sourceforge.net/projects/os-uninstaller/files/po.tar.gz/download") (now available in 10 languages! de/es/en/fr/it/nl/pt/sv/ta/tr)
- suggesting improvements
- looking at the code (mainly bash) : [https://sourceforge.net/projects/os-uninstaller/files/](https://sourceforge.net/projects/os-uninstaller/files/)

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-26
Just a quick update on "Boot Repair". I installed and ran it from a working Natty installation. When I chose to 'reinstall the Grub bootloader' it appeared to run grub-install and successfully reinstalled G2 to the MBR and updated the core.img file as it should.


[I][COLOR="Navy"]Edit: When electing to purge/reinstall Grub, read the instructions above the "Warning" screenshot. Open a terminal and run the instructions as described.
The rest of this post was the result of missing these instructions. The app works as it is supposed to.[/COLOR][/I]

When I tried the Advanced option to manually purge and reinstall G2, it ran but finished saying that the operation was aborted and no changes were made. I had selected to keep the same OS I was currently using (Natty). I can confirm this result as I watched the /etc/grub.d and /boot/grub folders during the operation. During a normal purge and reinstall, (almost all of) the files in these two folders are removed during the purge of grub-pc and grub-common. This did not happen when I used Boot Repair (and Boot Repair said it made no changes). 

For a complete purge/reinstall to work if files are corrupted, these files must be removed so they can be replaced by the originals.

I'd like others who try Boot Repair to post their experiences with this app. If we can confirm it works to purge and reinstall Grub2 it will be very helpful for those who aren't comfortable with the command line or who just prefer using a GUI application.

### Post by YannBuntu on 2011-05-26
Thank you drs305 for your feedback.

> **drs305 said:**
> saying that the operation was aborted and no changes were made.

When selecting the "purge" option, one should get 2 successive windows : the first asking the user to open a terminal and type a command to remove grub. The second to type a command to reinstall it. The software checks if GRUB has been removed when the user clicks "next" at the 1st window, and checks if it has been reinstalled when clicking "next" at the 2nd window. It also checks if your internet connexion is active, as it is needed to reinstall grub-pc package.
The message you had ("no changes were made") should have appeared only if you had clicked on "Cancel" at first window. If not, please ZIP and mail me your /var/log/clean folder (it contains the logs).

---

### Post by drs305 on 2011-05-27
> **YannBuntu said:**
> Why so often ? (are you a GRUB dev ?)

No, definitely not a dev! Just a tinkerer and troubleshooter. 

When I work Grub2 problems I usually try to make my Grub setup exactly like the user's setup. Since I have lots of tweaks for my system, I just purge/reinstall to get all the defaults back to original and then make the necessary changes. Often I have to change Grub versions as well, which I do via purging, changing the repositories, then reinstalling the applicable version. From a running system it's all quite easy.

I am adding the "Boot Repair" option to the first post. I was going to refer to your post above, but it would be good (if you haven't already) to make a new "How To" thread. It can look just like the one you posted above, plus anything else you think a user may want to know about the program.

It will also allow users with problems to ask you directly in thread. 

If you make one, give us the link and I'll put that in the first post of this thread.

Just a couple of quick questions you might need to address: 
Will it work from the LiveCD (after adding the repository and installing)?  
If they have a separate /boot or /home (especially /boot) and running the LiveCD, will they have to manually mount these partitions first if using the LiveCD using the command line or will the app take care of that.
What the "No backup" message means when starting Boot Repair.

---

### Post by YannBuntu on 2011-05-28
Hi,

I just created a new thread for "Boot-Repair" : [http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=10871917&postcount=1](http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=10871917&postcount=1) , thanks for your suggestion.

It works if there is a separate /home, but it is not compatible with separate /boot (I will try to add this functionnality in next release).

The "No bootsector backup" message refers to another functionality of Boot-Repair : 
As you may know, some computers are MBR locked (OEM protection that prevents Windows to start if the MBR is modified), so that when you install Ubuntu (by default it installs GRUB in the MBR of /sda) in dual-boot with Windows, you can have the bad surprise to see that Windows is in the GRUB menu but does not start any more.
That is one of the reasons why we (ubuntu-fr) also developped "Clean-Ubiquity" ([https://launchpad.net/~yannubuntu/+archive/clean-ubiquity](https://launchpad.net/~yannubuntu/+archive/clean-ubiquity) )

---

### Post by paulgami on 2011-09-14
I upgraded ubuntu-server 10.04 to 10.10 with no issues, so a couple reboots later I upgraded the 10.10 to 11.04 (both times using apt-get upgrade until there were no more upgrades then using the do-release-upgrade command).
The 10.10 to 11.04 upgrade went smoothly, as far as I could see.  No errors but when it rebooted at the end I was left with a grub command line.  Here's my boot-info script, if that makes any sense to anyone!

```
                  Boot Info Script 0.60    from 17 May 2011


============================= Boot Info Summary: ===============================

 => Grub2 (v1.97-1.98) is installed in the MBR of /dev/sda and looks at sector 
    1 of the same hard drive for core.img. core.img is at this location and 
    looks in partition 1 for /grub.

sda1: __________________________________________________________________________

    File system:       ext2
    Boot sector type:  -
    Boot sector info:  
    Operating System:  
    Boot files:        /grub/grub.cfg /grub/core.img

sda2: __________________________________________________________________________

    File system:       Extended Partition
    Boot sector type:  -
    Boot sector info:  

sda5: __________________________________________________________________________

    File system:       LVM2_member
    Boot sector type:  -
    Boot sector info:  

============================ Drive/Partition Info: =============================

Drive: sda _____________________________________________________________________

Disk /dev/sda: 26.8 GB, 26843545600 bytes
255 heads, 63 sectors/track, 3263 cylinders, total 52428800 sectors
Units = sectors of 1 * 512 = 512 bytes
Sector size (logical/physical): 512 bytes / 512 bytes

Partition  Boot  Start Sector    End Sector  # of Sectors  Id System

/dev/sda1    *          2,048       499,711       497,664  83 Linux
/dev/sda2             501,758    52,426,751    51,924,994   5 Extended
/dev/sda5             501,760    52,426,751    51,924,992  8e Linux LVM


"blkid" output: ________________________________________________________________

Device           UUID                                   TYPE       LABEL

/dev/loop0                                              squashfs   
/dev/sda1        76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2   ext2       
/dev/sda5        cRHPyX-urv8-1ELa-Je0D-z8Jn-HJML-lV90Bw LVM2_member 

================================ Mount points: =================================

Device           Mount_Point              Type       Options

/dev/loop0       /rofs                    squashfs   (ro,noatime)
/dev/sda1        /media/76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2 ext2       (rw,nosuid,nodev,uhelper=udisks)
/dev/sr0         /cdrom                   iso9660    (ro,noatime)


============================= sda1/grub/grub.cfg: ==============================

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
#
# DO NOT EDIT THIS FILE
#
# It is automatically generated by grub-mkconfig using templates
# from /etc/grub.d and settings from /etc/default/grub
#

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/00_header ###
if [ -s $prefix/grubenv ]; then
  set have_grubenv=true
  load_env
fi
set default="0"
if [ "${prev_saved_entry}" ]; then
  set saved_entry="${prev_saved_entry}"
  save_env saved_entry
  set prev_saved_entry=
  save_env prev_saved_entry
  set boot_once=true
fi

function savedefault {
  if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then
    saved_entry="${chosen}"
    save_env saved_entry
  fi
}

function recordfail {
  set recordfail=1
  if [ -n "${have_grubenv}" ]; then if [ -z "${boot_once}" ]; then save_env recordfail; fi; fi
}

function load_video {
  insmod vbe
}

insmod lvm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(webhost-root)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root f39e5318-eb6b-4200-ad57-8adb97590cae
if loadfont /usr/share/grub/unicode.pf2 ; then
  set gfxmode=auto
  load_video
  insmod gfxterm
fi
terminal_output gfxterm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2
set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
set locale_dir=($root)/grub/locale
set lang=en_US
insmod gettext
if [ "${recordfail}" = 1 ]; then
  set timeout=-1
else
  set timeout=10
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/00_header ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###
set menu_color_normal=white/black
set menu_color_highlight=black/light-gray
### END /etc/grub.d/05_debian_theme ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###
if [ ${recordfail} != 1 ]; then
  if [ -e ${prefix}/gfxblacklist.txt ]; then
    if hwmatch ${prefix}/gfxblacklist.txt 3; then
      if [ ${match} = 0 ]; then
        set linux_gfx_mode=keep
      else
        set linux_gfx_mode=text
      fi
    else
      set linux_gfx_mode=text
    fi
  else
    set linux_gfx_mode=keep
  fi
else
  set linux_gfx_mode=text
fi
export linux_gfx_mode
if [ "$linux_gfx_mode" != "text" ]; then load_video; fi
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.38-11-server' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
	recordfail
	set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	linux	/vmlinuz-2.6.38-11-server root=/dev/mapper/webhost-root ro   quiet
	initrd	/initrd.img-2.6.38-11-server
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.38-11-server (recovery mode)' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
	recordfail
	set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	echo	'Loading Linux 2.6.38-11-server ...'
	linux	/vmlinuz-2.6.38-11-server root=/dev/mapper/webhost-root ro single 
	echo	'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
	initrd	/initrd.img-2.6.38-11-server
}
submenu "Previous Linux versions" {
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.35-30-server' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
	recordfail
	set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	linux	/vmlinuz-2.6.35-30-server root=/dev/mapper/webhost-root ro   quiet
	initrd	/initrd.img-2.6.35-30-server
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.35-30-server (recovery mode)' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
	recordfail
	set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	echo	'Loading Linux 2.6.35-30-server ...'
	linux	/vmlinuz-2.6.35-30-server root=/dev/mapper/webhost-root ro single 
	echo	'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
	initrd	/initrd.img-2.6.35-30-server
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.32-33-server' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
	recordfail
	set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	linux	/vmlinuz-2.6.32-33-server root=/dev/mapper/webhost-root ro   quiet
	initrd	/initrd.img-2.6.32-33-server
}
menuentry 'Ubuntu, with Linux 2.6.32-33-server (recovery mode)' --class ubuntu --class gnu-linux --class gnu --class os {
	recordfail
	set gfxpayload=$linux_gfx_mode
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	echo	'Loading Linux 2.6.32-33-server ...'
	linux	/vmlinuz-2.6.32-33-server root=/dev/mapper/webhost-root ro single 
	echo	'Loading initial ramdisk ...'
	initrd	/initrd.img-2.6.32-33-server
}
}
### END /etc/grub.d/10_linux ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###
### END /etc/grub.d/20_linux_xen ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###
menuentry "Memory test (memtest86+)" {
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	linux16	/memtest86+.bin
}
menuentry "Memory test (memtest86+, serial console 115200)" {
	insmod part_msdos
	insmod ext2
	set root='(hd0,msdos1)'
	search --no-floppy --fs-uuid --set=root 76bbc9dc-b931-498f-a134-34c71a65cde2
	linux16	/memtest86+.bin console=ttyS0,115200n8
}
### END /etc/grub.d/20_memtest86+ ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###
if [ "x${timeout}" != "x-1" ]; then
  if keystatus; then
    if keystatus --shift; then
      set timeout=-1
    else
      set timeout=0
    fi
  else
    if sleep --interruptible 3 ; then
      set timeout=0
    fi
  fi
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/30_os-prober ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###
# This file provides an easy way to add custom menu entries.  Simply type the
# menu entries you want to add after this comment.  Be careful not to change
# the 'exec tail' line above.
### END /etc/grub.d/40_custom ###

### BEGIN /etc/grub.d/41_custom ###
if [ -f  $prefix/custom.cfg ]; then
  source $prefix/custom.cfg;
fi
### END /etc/grub.d/41_custom ###

### Post by drs305 on 2011-09-16
paulgami,

I'd like to help but I'm woefully uninformed about LVM, RAID, etc. This thread probably doesn't get read too often except by those needing help, so if you don't get a positive response you might want to start a new thread and include the partitioning scheme you are using in the title.

If you can identify the partition from the grub prompt, I'll give some general advice:

Since you now get the 'grub' prompt, it means that Grub 2 has probably found the grub boot files but for some reason can't use or find the grub.cfg file. 

From the grub prompt, if you use the "ls" command perhaps it will now identify your OS partition. If it does, try the following commands to boot. I'm going to use "hd0,1" in the example, but if found yours may be more exotic and certainly different. Substitute the correct value:

```
insmod (hd0,1)/boot/grug/normal.mod  # This may display the menu.
configfile (hd0,1)/boot/grub/grub.cfg  # This may also display the menu

```

If it does, you may need to press 'e' to edit the menuentry and check the lines for the proper addresses/format.

If you can find the correct partition with the "ls" command the above doesn't work, you can try (with substitutions):
```

set root=(hd0,1)
linux (hd0,1)/vmlinuz root=/dev/sda1 ro
initrd (hd0,1)/initrd.img
boot # or CTRL-x to boot.

```

---

### Post by oldfred on 2011-09-16
I also do not use LVM, but just like you installed the lvm2 package when you chrooted you need grub needs lvm.mod to see the lvm when booting?

From your grub.cfg file you posted. I would think you need these as part of a manual boot.

insmod lvm
insmod part_msdos
insmod ext2

I think grub automatically includes the second two but does not include lvm as standard.

### Post by drs305 on 2011-11-15
If you want to get Windows working again, and its files weren't changed, you should be able to restore it by booting the following command and pointing the MBR back to the Windows partition.

If Ubuntu is more important, or after you have Windows working again, we can see what happened if you will download and run the boot info script. Run it from the LiveCd and post the contents of RESULTS.txt. The script page is linked in my signature line (BIS).

To get Windows booting, from the LiveCD install an alternate bootloader, lilo. Then run the second command. There will be messages about lilo needing to be configured, but disregard these messages. Run just the two commands and reboot and you should see your Windows bootloader. 

If your drive is not sda, change the second command to the proper drive.
```
sudo apt-get install lilo
sudo lilo -M /dev/sd**a** mbr
```

---

### Post by YannBuntu on 2011-11-17
Hello Pranaykotasthane,
i think there are files missing in your /boot folder. Please connect internet, run Boot-Repair, click on "Advanced options", select "Purge and reinstall GRUB", and apply. When you will be asked to reinstall GRUB (in a blue screen), please put the star at the left-side of /sda, not /sda8. A new URL will appear at the end, indicate it to us if you still meet problems.

### Post by slowneutrino on 2012-01-25
Thanks! I was able to get old grub removed and grub2 installed and get Ubuntu 11.10 booted finally. 

I used the Live CD, and the chroot method. Some of the steps above got me past my own previous attempts: cp the /etc/resolv.conf to the existing disk location so networking in chroot would work, and the "mount --bind" commands to get /dev, etc. available in the chrooted environment. 

Other tricks I had to use before the above would work:
"apt-get install lvm2 mdadm" to be able to mount my old disk file systems (mirrored and LVM), then:

"mdadm -A /dev/md0; mdadm -A /dev/md1; mkdir /oldroot; mount /dev/vgmain/root /oldroot;" etc

---

    set gfxmode=auto

    insmod efi_uga
    insmod gfxterm
    terminal_output gfxterm
fi

    set gfxpayload=keep
    linux    /casper/vmlinuz  file=/cdrom/preseed/ubuntu.seed boot=casper quiet splash --
    initrd    /casper/initrd.lz
}
menuentry "Install Ubuntu" {
    set gfxpayload=keep
    linux    /casper/vmlinuz  file=/cdrom/preseed/ubuntu.seed boot=casper only-ubiquity quiet splash --
    initrd    /casper/initrd.lz
}
menuentry "Check disc for defects" {
    set gfxpayload=keep
    linux    /casper/vmlinuz  boot=casper integrity-check quiet splash --
    initrd    /casper/initrd.lz
}

### Post by drs305 on 2012-02-22
yelojakit,

Are you using the same version/architecture CD as your installation (i.e. 11.10/32 or 64 bit) to chroot?

Are you sure when you boot the LiveCD the drive is 'sda' and not something else. After mounting sda3, run "ls /mnt/temp/boot" to make sure it sees Ubuntu files.

Did you ever have the installed Ubuntu working? If not, it may be best just to retrieve any data you have on the partition and just reisntall the OS.

Your setup seems very simple (no separate /boot, /home, etc) so other than not following the commands those are the things that come readily to mind.

```

sudo mkdir /mnt/temp 
sudo mount /dev/sda3 /mnt/temp
for i in /dev /dev/pts /proc /sys; do sudo mount -B $i /mnt/temp$i;  done
sudo cp /etc/resolv.conf /mnt/temp/etc/resolv.conf
sudo chroot /mnt/temp

apt-get update
apt-get purge grub grub-pc grub-common
apt-get install grub-common grub-pc
update-grub 
exit

for i in /dev/pts /dev /proc /sys; do sudo umount /mnt/temp$i ; done

```

---

### Post by Vertoke on 2012-06-03
Hello my dear Sir Drs305,

Sorry to bring up an old thread. I'll be bold: I didn't read all 21 pages of the thread. But I do like to share some of my findings while trying to upgrade my Natty Narwhal (Ubuntu 11.04) *server* to the lateste Precise Pangolin LTS (Ubuntu 12.04) *server*. Maybe, by doing so, I could clarify some things to people who had a similar problem. Even bolder: you maybe able to update your HOWTO to help out pick the different a,b,c of the "chroot" -> "Mounting Instructions" step.

First of all let me thank you very very much dear Sir Drs305! For you have saved my precious home server from being re-installed. I would also like to thank every one related to the threads and posts I'll be referencing to. 

[SIZE="3"]In short:[/SIZE]
When you have a boot device that has **GPT** or **LVM** you can not use GRUB.
You need (or have) a **different boot partition** which has an MBR. Therefor you need to take **step A** in the guide.
*Please refer to the bottom of the long version for details [COLOR="Red"]*[/COLOR]*

[SIZE="3"]The long version[/SIZE]
My story started off in summer 2008 when I bought a cheap but nice and energy efficient PC configuration for a home server. Back then I used [[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]FreeNAS[/COLOR]]("http://www.freenas.org/") v6, later v7 and I tried v8 for 3 days. This was fine for a while but at a cerntain point I found FreeNAS somewhat to limited for what I wanted. I had modified and played with it a lot. I was very happy about FreeNAS for what it had to offer as a NAS OS. But I needed more than just a NAS system.

Soo I switched over to Ubuntu server edition. It had everything I needed, and a lot more. In 2008 I was very new to *nix-based systems, but that had changed. I installed Ubuntu Server 11.04 (Natty Narwhal). From what I had learned, and under 'influence' of  my best friend, a true SysAdmin, I used different **partitions** and even used **LVM2**. It was a whole new world opening to me. I really love Ubuntu by now. I could play to my full and complete hearts content.

That was over a year ago. A few weeks ago, and again under 'influence' of that true SysAdmin, I started upgrading my Natty Narwhal Server to Precise Pangolin Server. That, and again more experience, had made me realize I had made a "beauty mistake" with the naming of my LVM2 volumes. **The system LVM2 volume group** was named "Narwhal", after the OS name. I wanted to change that LVM2 volume group name and I did. But there I was ignorant about booting and more precise (no pun intended) about GRUB.

The boot device, which had LVM2 on it, had its LVM2 volume group name changed. That was something GRUB could not handle by just a configuration change. I had it working, before on Natty Nawhal with LVM2. I changed the volume groupname and every reference to it including for boot but it didn't work... why?

It took just under 6 days to get to the bottom of this, of course with a lot of trying and trail and error. I learned about the diffence between GRUB and GRUB2. I found out about GRUB loading stage 1.5 error 15. I had a struggle because of the LVM2 and because I didn't knew about chroot I messed up live USB's. 

But yesterday just before dinner I got my  Precise Pangolin LTS (Ubuntu 12.04) booting again. It was back better than ever before. *I had, again, learned very much because of the forums here and Google'n*.

[LIST]
[*]GRUB (still?) can not handle booting from big volumes **(LVM2)** and **GPT**. You need a separate boot partition with MBR.
[*]You can not combine two **different versions of GRUB**!
[*]When using a LiveUSB of other Live system to recover your booting you realy need to **chroot**
[*]Because I have the server version it is normaly headless and I do every thing in commandline. therefor I didn't realy look into [[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]Boot-repair[/COLOR]]("https://help.ubuntu.com/community/Boot-Repair"), sorry.
[*]I did however tried [[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]Rescatux[/COLOR]]("http://www.supergrubdisk.org/rescatux/") but I am sorry to tell you, from my experince, it didn't help one bit. Probaly because of the GPT and LVM2.
[/LIST]

The last two list items are self explanatory. But for the others I would like to add some extra information.

*Thank you drs305* for pointing out the **chroot**. This was a command I didn't use before and I learned a very valuable lesson by screwing up my **liveUSB** a few times because of not using chroot. I may sound dumb, and I sure did dumb things this past few days, but because of you my euro (penny) dropped and I could fix GRUB(2) from liveUSB.

I am a big fan of trail and error. Because of that I ended up installing the **older version of GRUB together with GRUB2**. That doesn't work! It will give you:
```
GRUB loading stage 1.5

GRUB loading, please wait...
Error 15
```
Read more about this in the following thread: [[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]GRUB loading stage 1.5 error 15[/COLOR]]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1612068")

> **oldfred said:**
> Error 15 is often a conflict between old grub & grub2. Although grub legacy was supposed to let you boot and then boot into grub2. (As your menu.lst shows)

You also have wubi in your windows partiiton. Were/are you using wubi?

Thank you very much **missfroguk** and **oldfred** for helping me with this!

[COLOR="Red"][SIZE="3"]*[/SIZE][/COLOR] For the very first item on the list I would like to refer to a blog post from [[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]Shuvo[/COLOR]]("http://www.shuvoovuhs.com/author/shuvo/"). He pointed out that you need a MBR partition to boot up with GRUB. Thank you very much Shuvo!

[[COLOR="RoyalBlue"]GRUB Installation Issue with 2 TB HDD &#8211; GPT Requires BIOS Boot Partition[/COLOR]]("http://www.shuvoovuhs.com/linux/grub-installation-issue-with-2-tb-hdd-gpt-requires-bios-boot-partition/")

Because I had GRUB2 already working on the Natty Narwhal Server that install made my **separate boot partition with MBR**.
It was only after I realized I needed a different boot partition that I could solve my boot problem for my Precise Pangolin Server.

[SIZE="3"]The point:[/SIZE]
**When you work with GPT, LVM2 or big disks you must have a separate boot partition that has an MBR**.
This makes a difference when you are following the great guide at the start of this post. Because you have a different boot partition you need to **take step A** when mounting for chroot.


I hope my post can help others who face problems with there GRUB together with GPT or LVM.

[I]Kindest regards
Vertoke[/I]

### Post by drs305 on 2012-06-03

### Post by oldfred on 2012-06-03
Partitioning with gpt or MBR both have a MBR, which is the first sector of the hard drive. With gpt it is a protective MBR so older partitioning tools like fdisk do not damage the partitioning.

With Ubuntu you can use gpt and boot with BIOS but the sectors just after the MBR that grub2 uses to embed the core.img is not there. So with gpt you have to have a 1MB partition, unformated with bios_grub flag. Windows will only boot from gpt partitioned drives if UEFI booting not BIOS.

Do not know much about LVM but grub2 has lvm.mod and works directly to boot from LVM, grub legacy did not. But separate /boot partition may still make sense. With Ubuntu liveCD you also have to download lvm2 driver to get it to work with lvm partitioning. Normally alternative installer or server installing include the LVM drivers.

Most desktops do not have LVM as it is more advanced.
Advantages/Disadvantages Post #9
[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1586328](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1586328)

I just used the purge & reinstall on my gpt drive without any issue, but I boot with BIOS. And I have a bios_grub partition. 
Ubuntu used to install to gpt drives but use blocklists like an install to a partition, but I guess now it wants the bios_grub partition so it can install correctly.

---

### Post by YannBuntu on 2012-06-04
**@Vertoke:** if you don't use a separate /usr ([not supported yet]("https://bugs.launchpad.net/boot-repair/+bug/1008469"), i'm working on it), you can use Boot-Repair even if you want to repair the GRUB of a Ubuntu server. It handles LVM, GPT and separate /boot.
In case the "Recommended repair" doesn't fix GRUB correctly, please indicate the URL that will appear.
